A dark figure, with a gun, had seen him\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95was making for him, from\r\ndown the deck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHist, Peter!\xe2\x80\x9d huskily spoke a voice. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis Pat. Ye\xe2\x80\x99re all right. Stay\r\nwhere ye are, now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYes, except for Pat, the sentry, all the big boat was deserted.\r\nThere was a great time ashore. Crouched panting and dripping, Peter\r\nwitnessed, from behind the mast. The shore was bright, the figures\r\nplainly outlined. There were the two white chiefs. Of this he was\r\ncertain. They had on their heads the queer hats; they wore long tight\r\nblue shirts that glittered with ornaments; they carried the long\r\nknives, in sheathes at their sides; the one was the chief with the\r\nyellow hair, and the other was the chief with the red hair.\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x99Nited States were giving a feast and dance, evidently. Two of them\r\nwere making music by drawing a stick across a box held to their chins;\r\nand the others, and the Indians, sat in a circle, around the fire,\r\nwatching the dances.\r\n\r\nIt was now the turn of the Otoes, for they sprang up, and into the\r\ncentre, to dance. Peter knew them, one by one: Head Chief Little Thief,\r\nBig Horse, Crow\xe2\x80\x99s Head, Black Cat, Iron Eyes, Bix Ox, Brave Man, and\r\nBig Blue Eyes\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95all Otoes except Crow\xe2\x80\x99s Head and Black Cat, who were\r\nMissouris.\r\n\r\nThey danced. It was the Oto Buffalo Dance. The \xe2\x80\x99Nited States warriors\r\ncheered\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and on a sudden cheered louder and clapped their hands\r\ntogether, for into the centre had leaped a new figure, to dance by\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\nHe was the black medicine man!\r\n\r\nHis eyes rolled white; his teeth were white; but all the rest of him\r\nwas black\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and he was very large. Assuredly, the \xe2\x80\x99Nited States must be\r\na great and powerful nation, with such medicine men, decided little\r\nPeter, watching.\r\n\r\nAlong the deck Patrick Gass hissed and beckoned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere,\xe2\x80\x9d he bade. Peter scurried to him. \xe2\x80\x9cGet down in for\xe2\x80\x99d,\xe2\x80\x9d and Pat\r\npointed to the open door of the forecastle or wooden house that had\r\nbeen built in the bows, under a higher deck. \xe2\x80\x9cStow yourself away an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nkape quiet. Ye\xe2\x80\x99ll find a place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeter darted in. It was a room lined with beds in tiers from floor\r\nto ceiling: the white warriors\xe2\x80\x99 sleeping-room. Clothing was hanging\r\nagainst the far end; down the centre was a narrow table. Like a cat\r\nagain, Peter sprang upon the table, scrambled into the highest of the\r\nbunks on this side, and came to the far-end wall. The wall did not\r\nmeet the roof; it was a bulkhead partition dividing off the room from\r\nthe remainder of the bows. Peter thrust his arm in over the top, and\r\ncould feel, there beyond, a solid bale on a level with the bunk. He\r\nwriggled in over, landed cautiously, explored with hands and feet, in\r\nthe darkness\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and stretched out in a space that had been left between\r\nthe ballast of extra supplies and the deck above. Good!\r\n\r\nThat warm August night the \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Nited States\xe2\x80\x9d men of Captains Lewis and\r\nClark slept on the sand, in the open air, by the river; and in the\r\ntent of the captains slept Chief Little Thief. But Patrick Gass, when\r\nrelieved from guard duty, slept in the forecastle, near Peter\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95that\r\nbeing, as he yawned, \xe2\x80\x9cmore convanient.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII\r\n\r\nPETER MEETS THE CHIEFS\r\n\r\n\r\nThe hour was early when Pat stuck his head over the partition, and to\r\nPeter said: \xe2\x80\x9cWhisht! Are ye awake, Peter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99lo,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Peter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch ye a bite to eat, an\xe2\x80\x99 wather to drink,\xe2\x80\x9d said Patrick. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99\r\nye best lie hid till we start, when the Injuns go. \xe2\x80\x99Twon\xe2\x80\x99t be long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAw-right,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Peter.\r\n\r\nPatrick passed in to him some dried meat and a canteen of water. After\r\nthat the day seemed to move very slowly. Here on the boat all was\r\nquiet, particularly in Peter\xe2\x80\x99s end. However, outside on the shore there\r\nwas a constant sound of voices, from the \xe2\x80\x99Nited States camp.\r\n\r\nThe sun rose high, as betokened by the close warmth where Peter lay\r\nhidden. He felt as though he must get out and see what was going on. So\r\nhe peered over the top of the partition, to find whether the forecastle\r\nwas empty. It was. He slipped down into it, and stealing through and\r\nworming flat across the deck, peeped through a crack in the gunwale.\r\n\r\nLittle Thief and his Otoes and Missouris had not yet gone. They were\r\nholding another council with the \xe2\x80\x99Nited States. More talk! The \xe2\x80\x99Nited\r\nStates chiefs and warriors were sitting, and the Otoes and Missouris\r\nwere sitting, all forming a great circle.\r\n\r\nOne after another the Otoes and the Missouris arose and talked, and the\r\nwhite chiefs replied; but of all this talk Peter understood little.\r\nAfter a time he grew tired; the sun was hot, and he went back into his\r\nnook. He still had meat and water enough.\r\n\r\nIt was much later when he awakened, to hear people in the room beyond\r\nhis partition. There were white men\xe2\x80\x99s voices\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95one voice sounded like\r\nthat of his other friend, George Shannon. And there were groans.\r\nSoon the white men left\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95all except the man who groaned. He stayed.\r\nEvidently one of the white men was sick, and had been put into a bed.\r\n\r\nDusk was falling, and Peter thought that he might venture out and\r\nstretch his legs. The sounds from the sick man had ceased; maybe\r\nhe slept. Peter peered over. Everything was quiet; and forth he\r\nslipped\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95only to discover that in the open door was sitting, amidst the\r\ndusk, a watcher. It was the United States warrior, George Shannon. He\r\nsaw Peter, poised about to leap down, and smiled and beckoned. Peter\r\nlightly went to him.\r\n\r\nGeorge Shannon looked worn and anxious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you all right, Peter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Aw-right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA soldier\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95very sick,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, and pointed to a bunk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat name?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Peter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharles Floyd. He danced and got hot. Lay down on the sand all night\r\nand got cold. Now very sick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh,\xe2\x80\x9d grunted Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cMebbe get well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said George, soberly.\r\n\r\nThat was too bad. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t they call in the black medicine-man?\r\n\r\nExcept for George and the sick Charles Floyd, the boat was deserted;\r\nfor on the shore another dance and feast were in progress. Chief Little\r\nThief and his Indians were staying, and the \xe2\x80\x99Nited States appeared to\r\nbe bent upon giving them a good time.\r\n\r\nAll that night the sick Charles Floyd moaned at intervals, in the bunk;\r\nand George Shannon and Patrick Gass and others kept watch over him;\r\nwhile Peter, on the other side of the partition, listened or slept.\r\nToward morning, when Peter next woke up, he had been aroused by tramp\r\nof feet over his head, and splash of water against the boat, and orders\r\nshouted, and a movement of the boat itself.\r\n\r\nThey were starting, and he was starting with them! Hoorah! Now he was\r\nnot hungry or thirsty or tired; he was excited.\r\n\r\nYes, the boat was moving. He could hear the plashing of oars, and the\r\ncreak as the sail was raised. And in a few minutes more the boat leaned\r\nand swerved and tugged, and the river rippled under its bow.\r\n\r\nPeter waited as long as he possibly could stand it to wait. Patrick\r\nGass had said for him to lie hidden until Chief Little Thief had left,\r\nand the boat had started. Very well.\r\n\r\nAll was silent in the room beyond. He peered, and could see nobody.\r\nOver the partition he once more squirmed, into the top-most bunk; and\r\nfeeling with his toes let himself down. The door was shut, but it had a\r\nwindow in it that he might look out of; and if anybody opened, he would\r\ndive under the table or under a bunk, until he saw who it was.\r\n\r\nThe sick man in the bottom bunk opposite suddenly exclaimed. He was\r\nawake and watching.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are you?\xe2\x80\x9d he challenged weakly.\r\n\r\nWith his feet on the floor, Peter paused, to stare. He saw a pale,\r\nclammy countenance gazing at him from the blanket coverings\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and at\r\nthat instant the door opened, and before Peter might so much as stir,\r\nthe chief with the red hair entered. Peter was fairly caught. He drew\r\nbreath sharply, and resolved not to show fear.\r\n\r\nThe chief with the red hair was all in buckskin, and wore moccasins on\r\nhis feet, and on his head a round hat with the brim looped up in front.\r\nHis face was without hair and was very tanned, so that it was reddish\r\nbrown instead of white, and his two eyes were clear, keen gray. His\r\nhair was bound behind in a long bag of thin skin. He had rather a large\r\nnose, and a round chin; and was heavy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell!\xe2\x80\x9d he uttered. He glanced swiftly from Peter to the sick man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbunk, and back again to Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe stole down from above, Captain,\xe2\x80\x9d said the sick man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you, Sergeant? Any better?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir. I\xe2\x80\x99m awful weak, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch pain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been suffering terribly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, my man. We\xe2\x80\x99ll do all we can for you.\xe2\x80\x9d Now the chief spoke\r\nto Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cWho are you? How\xe2\x80\x99d you come here?\xe2\x80\x9d His voice was stern and\r\nquick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hide,\xe2\x80\x9d said Peter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeter pointed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho brought you here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI come. Night. Swim down river. Hide.\xe2\x80\x9d For Peter had no notion of\r\ntelling on Patrick Gass and George Shannon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHumph! You did!\xe2\x80\x9d And the chief with the red hair grunted. \xe2\x80\x9cRan away,\r\neh? Who was your chief?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe-ah-rush-hah. First Osage, then Oto, but me white.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s your mother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeter shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s your father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeter shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s a pretty pickle,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the chief with the red hair\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and\r\nPeter wondered what he meant. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you come along with me.\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\nadded, to the sick man, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be back directly, Charley; as soon as\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve turned this stow-away over. Do you want anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir. I\xe2\x80\x99m sleepy. Maybe I\xe2\x80\x99ll sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d and the sick man\xe2\x80\x99s voice\r\ntrailed off into a murmur.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here,\xe2\x80\x9d bade the red-haired chief to Peter, beckoning with his\r\nfinger. And Peter followed Captain William Clark, of the United States\r\nArtillery, and second in command of this Captains Lewis and Clark\r\ngovernment exploring expedition up the Missouri River, through the\r\ndoorway, into the sunshine and the open of the great barge\xe2\x80\x99s deck.\r\n\r\nCaptain Clark led straight for the stern, but on the way Peter, keeping\r\nclose behind him, with his quick eyes saw many things. The white\r\nwarriors, in buckskins or in cloth, were busy here and there, mending\r\nclothes and tools and weapons and assorting goods, or viewing the river\r\nbanks\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and all paused to gaze at him. The big sail was pulling lustily,\r\nfrom its mast. At the stern two warriors were steering. In the barge\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwake were sailing the two smaller barges, the red one and the white\r\none. They followed gallantly, the river rippled, the banks were flowing\r\npast. Nothing was to be seen moving on the banks, and the site of the\r\nOmaha village, and the sand sprit where the council with Little Thief\r\nhad been held, were gone. Good!\r\n\r\nBefore the cabin in the stern of the barge were standing the slim,\r\nyellow-haired chief and Patrick Gass, and they were watching Peter\r\ncoming. The slim chief was dressed in his blue clothes and his odd\r\nhat, and wore his long knife by his side. His hair hung in a tail.\r\nPatrick Gass was dressed as always. His eyes twinkled at Peter, as if\r\nto say: \xe2\x80\x9cNow, what are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeter knew what he was going to do. He was going to stay with the\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Nited States.\r\n\r\nBut the slim chief\xe2\x80\x99s face betrayed no sign. He simply waited. For this\r\nCaptain Meriwether Lewis, of the First United States Infantry, the\r\nleader of the exploring expedition sent out by President Jefferson and\r\nCongress, was not much given to smiles, and was strong on discipline. A\r\nthorough young soldier, he, who felt the heavy responsibility of taking\r\nthe expedition safely through, with the help of Captain Clark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99ve found, Merne,\xe2\x80\x9d announced Captain Clark, with half a\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is he, Will?\xe2\x80\x9d Captain Lewis\xe2\x80\x99s query was quick, and his brows\r\nknitted a trifle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says he\xe2\x80\x99s white. I found him in the forec\xe2\x80\x99sle when I went in to see\r\nabout Floyd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is Floyd?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99d that boy get there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRan away from the Otoes, he says, and hid himself in the bows beyond\r\nthe bulkhead. Like as not he\xe2\x80\x99s been there a day or two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded the Long Knife Chief, of Peter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeter\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95Kerr.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOto. No like Oto. No like Indian. White boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHah! Did the Otoes steal you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOsage. Oto buy me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did the Osage get you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95not\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Peter, slowly, trying to speak the right words.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKill\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95father. Take mother. She die. Long time ago. Me\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95I white.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, Captain, didn\xe2\x80\x99t we hear down St. Louis way of a family by the\r\nsame name o\xe2\x80\x99 Kerr bein\xe2\x80\x99 wiped out by the Injuns some years back,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke\r\nPatrick Gass, saluting. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas up country a bit, though I disremember\r\nwhere, sorr.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but there was no boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was a bit of a baby, seems to me like, sorr,\xe2\x80\x9d alleged Sergeant\r\nGass. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 the woman was carried off, sorr.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis shrugged his shoulders impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, Pat. You go forward and you and Shannon see if you can\r\ndo anything for Floyd. Don\xe2\x80\x99t let him move much. He\xe2\x80\x99s liable to be\r\nrestless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sorr.\xe2\x80\x9d Patrick Gass saluted but lingered a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I might be\r\nso bold, sorr\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeein\xe2\x80\x99 as how the boy\xe2\x80\x99s Irish\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIrish! He\xe2\x80\x99s as black as an Indian!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sorr. But the eyes an\xe2\x80\x99 hair of him, sorr. An\xe2\x80\x99 sure he has an\r\nIrish name. An\xe2\x80\x99 I was thinkin\xe2\x80\x99, beggin\xe2\x80\x99 your pardon, sorr, if you\r\ndecided to kape him a spell, Shannon an\xe2\x80\x99 me\xe2\x80\x99d look after him for ye,\r\nsorr. We Irish are all cousins, ye know, sorr.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYoung Captain Lewis\xe2\x80\x99s mouth twitched; he shot a glance at Captain\r\nClark, who smiled back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes that sound to you like an Irish name, Captain? More like good old\r\nEnglish, to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was thinkin\xe2\x80\x99 again, sorr,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Pat, \xe2\x80\x9cthat more like it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_O_\xe2\x80\x99Kerr.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will do, Gass. Go forward and find Shannon, and the two of\r\nyou tend to Floyd.\xe2\x80\x9d Patrick saluted and trudged away. Captain Lewis\r\ncontinued, to Captain Clark: \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s something back of this, Will.\r\nGass is too willing. I\xe2\x80\x99ll wager he and Shannon know more than we do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s the Irish in him, Merne. Do you think they smuggled the lad\r\naboard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf they did\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95who brought you on this boat?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded the Long Knife\r\nChief of Peter.\r\n\r\nPeter shrugged _his_ shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI come,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo with \xe2\x80\x99Nited States. Up big river.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho taught you to speak English?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95mother,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cNo English; \xe2\x80\x99Merican; Ken-tuck-y.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKentuckian!\xe2\x80\x9d blurted Captain Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is white, sure enough. That\r\ncomes pretty close to home-folks, Merne. I know some Kerrs there,\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the question is, what are we to do with him?\xe2\x80\x9d reminded Captain\r\nLewis, sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t cumber ourselves with useless baggage, and we\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t start out by stealing children from the Indians.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; and yet it sort of goes against the grain to let the Indians keep\r\nany children they\xe2\x80\x99ve stolen,\xe2\x80\x9d argued Captain Clark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I agree with you there, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the\r\nPresident instructed us to make friends with all the tribes. We could\r\nhave shown the Otoes they were wrong, and could have offered to buy the\r\nboy or have made them promise to send him to St. Louis if we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsend him ourselves. This looks like bad faith.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we stop and put him ashore, Merne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we put you ashore will you go back to We-ah-rush-hah?\xe2\x80\x9d queried the\r\nLong Knife Chief, of Peter.\r\n\r\nPeter had not comprehended all that had been said, but he had listened\r\nanxiously\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and now he did understand that they were talking of putting\r\nhim off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cNo go back to We-ah-rush-hah. \xe2\x80\x99Maha catch me;\r\nSioux catch me; Oto whip me. No Indian; white.\xe2\x80\x9d And he added: \xe2\x80\x9cI follow\r\nboat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you give the order, Merne, we\xe2\x80\x99ll stop and send him back with an\r\nescort,\xe2\x80\x9d teased Captain Clark, who knew very well that Captain Lewis\r\nwould do no such thing. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd we\xe2\x80\x99ll tell the Otoes to forward him on\r\ndown to St. Louis. You think they\xe2\x80\x99d do it, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis tapped uneasily with his foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, pshaw, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t stop and waste this fine breeze,\r\neven to send back a boy. When we land for dinner will be the proper\r\ntime. We may meet some traders, bound down, and he can be started back\r\nwith them, to St. Louis. Meanwhile Gass and Shannon must take care of\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe can be sent down river with the first party that take back the\r\ndispatches,\xe2\x80\x9d proffered Captain Clark.\r\n\r\nPatrick Gass came clumping up the deck and again saluted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSergeant Floyd wishes might he speak with Cap\xe2\x80\x99n Clark, sorrs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is he, Pat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurrible weak, sorr, but the pain be not so bad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo ahead, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d bade Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cYou enlisted him. He knows you\r\nbetter. If I can do anything, call me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Red Hair Chief hastened away. The Long Knife Chief spoke to Patrick\r\nGass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll take charge of Peter until we send him back, Patrick. Draw on\r\nthe commissary for such clothes as he needs. We can\xe2\x80\x99t have him running\r\naround naked, this way, if he\xe2\x80\x99s white.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYis, sorr,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Patrick Gass. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, Peter, lad; come with your\r\ncousin Pat, an\xe2\x80\x99 we\xe2\x80\x99ll make your outside as white as your inside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeter gladly obeyed. He was rather afraid of the handsome young Long\r\nKnife Chief, but he was not afraid of Patrick Gass\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95no, nor of the Red\r\nHair.\r\n\r\nWhen dressed in the clothes that Patrick found for him, Peter was a\r\nfunny sight. There was a red flannel shirt\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95to Peter very beautiful,\r\nbut twice enough for him, so that the sleeves were rolled to their\r\nelbows, and the neck dropped about his shoulders. And there was a pair\r\nof blue trousers, also twice enough for him, so that the legs were\r\nrolled to their knees, and the waist was drawn up about his chest, and\r\nthe front doubled across where it was belted in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNiver you mind,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Patrick, while the \xe2\x80\x99Nited States men gazed on\r\nPeter and howled with merriment. \xe2\x80\x9cSure, I\xe2\x80\x99m a bit of a tailor an\xe2\x80\x99 if\r\nwe can\xe2\x80\x99t fit you with cloth we\xe2\x80\x99ll fit you with leather. Let \xe2\x80\x99em laugh.\r\nLaughin\xe2\x80\x99s good for the stomick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Peter did not mind. These were white people\xe2\x80\x99s clothes, and he was\r\nproud to wear them, although they did seem queer.\r\n\r\nThe sun had passed the overhead. At some orders the barge was swung in\r\nfor shore; the two smaller boats followed. Now would he be sent back,\r\nor left; or\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95what? Landing was made on the right-hand side, which was\r\nthe country of the Iowas and of the Sioux: not a good place, Peter\r\nreflected, for _him_. But scarcely had the barge tied up, and Peter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheart was beating with anxiety, when Captain Clark hastily emerged from\r\nthe forecastle; another soldier trod close behind.\r\n\r\nCaptain Clark went to Captain Lewis; the soldier proceeded slowly,\r\nspeaking to comrades. He arrived where Patrick was keeping friendly\r\nguard over Peter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharley\xe2\x80\x99s gone,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, simply, his face clouded, his voice broken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRest his soul in pace,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Patrick. \xe2\x80\x9cSure, I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, Nat. Did\r\nhe say anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe knew. He asked the Captain to write a letter for him, to the folks\r\nat home. After that he went to sleep and did not wake again, here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, he gave his life for his country,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted Patrick.\r\n\r\nSo the sick man had died. This much Peter easily guessed. It turned\r\ndinner into a very quiet affair. Nothing more was said of leaving Peter\r\nashore, nor of sending him back; but as soon as the dinner was finished\r\nthe boats all pushed out and headed up river, along a bank surmounted\r\nby rolling bluffs.\r\n\r\nAfter about a mile by sail and oars, everybody landed; and the body of\r\nSergeant Charles Floyd, United States Army, the first of the expedition\r\nto fall, was buried on the top of a bluff. Captain Clark read some\r\nwords out of a book, over the grave; and upon the grave was set a cedar\r\npost with the name, Sergt. C. Floyd, and the date, Aug. 20, 1804,\r\ncarved into it. Then three volleys from the rifles were fired.\r\n\r\nThe boats proceeded on for a camping-place, which was found about a\r\nmile up, on the right-hand or north side, near the mouth of a little\r\nriver. The bluff of the grave was referred to as Floyd\xe2\x80\x99s Bluff, and\r\nthe little river was called Floyd\xe2\x80\x99s River.\r\n\r\nAll the men, including Peter, felt sorry for Sergeant Nathaniel Pryor.\r\nFloyd had been his cousin. They felt sorry for those other relatives\r\nand friends, back at the Floyd home in Kentucky.\r\n\r\nFifty years later, or in 1857, the grave of the sergeant was moved a\r\nfew hundred feet, by the Sioux City, Iowa, people, so that it should\r\nnot crumble into the Missouri River; and in 1895 a monument was placed\r\nover it. To-day Floyd\xe2\x80\x99s Bluff is part of a Sioux City park.\r\n\r\nThe camp this evening was only thirteen miles above the Omaha village\r\nand the place where Chief Little Thief had come in to council, so that\r\nPeter very easily might have been sent back. But the death of Sergeant\r\nCharles Floyd seemed to be occupying the thoughts of the two captains;\r\nit made the whole camp sober. To-night there was no dancing or music,\r\nand Peter slept aboard the barge with nobody paying especial attention\r\nto him. Of this he was glad, because he feared that, once ashore, he\r\nwould be left behind\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95the \xe2\x80\x99Nited States would try to sail on without\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV\r\n\r\nTO THE LAND OF THE SIOUX\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFust we have to pass the Sioux Injuns,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Patrick Gass, to\r\nPeter. \xe2\x80\x9cYe know the Sioux?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey bad,\xe2\x80\x9d nodded Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cFight other Injuns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYis,\xe2\x80\x9d said Patrick. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we aim to make everybody paceful with\r\neverybody else. An\xe2\x80\x99 after the Sioux, we talk with the \xe2\x80\x99Rikaras.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Rees bad, too,\xe2\x80\x9d nodded Peter. For the Otoes were afraid of the\r\nnorthern tribes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYis,\xe2\x80\x9d said Patrick. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 after the \xe2\x80\x99Rikaras we come, I\xe2\x80\x99m thinkin\xe2\x80\x99, to\r\nthe Mandans, an\xe2\x80\x99 by that time \xe2\x80\x99twill be winter, an\xe2\x80\x99 with the Mandans\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll stay. I hear tell they have white skins an\xe2\x80\x99 blue eyes an\xe2\x80\x99 their\r\nhair trails on the ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSometimes sailing, sometimes rowed, and sometimes towed by heavy ropes\r\non which the men hauled, from the banks, the three boats had been\r\nsteadily advancing up-river. Peter was feeling quite at home. Everybody\r\nwas kind to him\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95especially Pat, who had been elected sergeant in place\r\nof Charles Floyd, and young George Shannon, who was only seventeen.\r\n\r\nTwo horses followed the boats, by land, for the use of the hunters.\r\nGeorge Drouillard, a Frenchman, who had lived with the Omahas, was\r\nchief hunter. At the evening camps Pierre Cruzatte, a merry Frenchman\r\nwith only one eye, and a soldier by the name of George Gibson, played\r\nlively music on stringed boxes called violins. Each night the two\r\ncaptains, and Pat and other soldiers, wrote on paper the story of\r\nthe trip. York, the black man, was Captain Clark\xe2\x80\x99s servant. Early in\r\nthe morning a horn was blown to arouse the camp. During the days the\r\ncaptains frequently went ashore, to explore.\r\n\r\nIt was well, thought Peter, that Pierre Dorion, a trader who lived with\r\nthe Sioux, was aboard the boats, for the fierce Sioux Indians did not\r\nlike strangers. Still, who could whip the United States?\r\n\r\nIn the afternoon of the eighth day after leaving Chief Little Thief,\r\nold Pierre, from where he was standing with the two captains on the\r\nbarge and gazing right and left and before, cried aloud and pointed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDere she is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, Dorion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDe Jacques, w\xe2\x80\x99at is also call de Yankton River; my people de Yankton\r\nSioux lif on her. Mebbe soon now we see some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe barge, flying its white peace flag, bordered with red and blue,\r\nploughed on. All eyes aboard were directed intently before. The mouth\r\nof the river gradually opened, amidst the trees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll halt there for dinner,\xe2\x80\x9d ordered Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cThat looks like\r\na good landing-place just above the mouth, Will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Clark nodded, and the barge began to veer in; the two pirogues\r\nor smaller boats imitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see one Injun,\xe2\x80\x9d said Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see him, Pat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere, now?\xe2\x80\x9d invited Patrick Gass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is standing still; watch us, this side of Yankton River.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, you\xe2\x80\x99ve sharp eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d praised Pat, squinting. \xe2\x80\x9cYis, sure I see\r\nhim, by the big tree just above the mouth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOthers saw him. And as the barge hove to, and led by Captain Clark the\r\nmen leaped for the shore, to cook dinner, the Indian plunged into the\r\nwater and swam across.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Maha!\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Peter, quickly, when, dripping, the Indian had plashed\r\nout and was boldly entering the camp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, is he, now?\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Patrick Gass.\r\n\r\nPierre Dorion translated for him, to the captains. He said that he was\r\nan Omaha boy, living with the Sioux. While he was talking, two other\r\nIndians came in. They indeed were Sioux\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95straight, dark, and dignified,\r\nas befitted members of a great and powerful nation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDey say de Yanktons, many of dem, are camp\xe2\x80\x99 to de west, one short\r\ntravel,\xe2\x80\x9d interpreted Dorion. \xe2\x80\x9cDey haf hear of our comin\xe2\x80\x99, an\xe2\x80\x99 will be\r\nplease\xe2\x80\x99 to meet de white chiefs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, Dorion. You go to the camp with these fellows, and tell the\r\nchiefs that we\xe2\x80\x99ll hold council at the river. I\xe2\x80\x99ll send Sergeant Pryor\r\nand another man along with you,\xe2\x80\x9d instructed Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nfind us again about opposite where their camp is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood,\xe2\x80\x9d approved Pierre Dorion. \xe2\x80\x9cNow mebbe I get my wife an\xe2\x80\x99 fam\xe2\x80\x99ly one\r\ntime more. My son, he dere, too, say dese young men.\xe2\x80\x9d For Pierre had\r\nmarried a Sioux woman.\r\n\r\nThe two Sioux, and Pierre, and Sergeant Nathaniel Pryor and Private\r\nJohn Potts left on foot for the camp of the Yanktons; but the Omaha boy\r\nstayed. Peter preferred to keep away from him. The Omahas, to him, were\r\nnot to be trusted.\r\n\r\nFrom the mouth of the Yankton River, which is to-day called the James\r\nRiver of South Dakota, the boats continued on up the Missouri, to the\r\ncouncil ground. The red pirogue ran upon a snag, so that it almost sank\r\nbefore it could be beached. Then all the goods had to be transferred to\r\nthe white pirogue. This took time, and it was not until nearly sunset\r\nthat Captain Lewis ordered landing to be made and camp pitched.\r\n\r\nThe camp of the Sioux was supposed to be somewhere across the river. In\r\nthe morning no Sioux had yet appeared for council, and Captain Lewis\r\nanxiously swept the country to the north with his spy-glass. However,\r\nIndians could not be hurried, as Peter well knew. But about four\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock there spread a murmur.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere they come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDe Sioux! Dey come. Now for beeg talk an\xe2\x80\x99 beeg dance! Hoo-zah!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOui!\xe2\x80\x9d added George Drouillard, the hunter. \xe2\x80\x9cMebbe fat dog feast, too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, murther!\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Pat. And, to Peter: \xe2\x80\x9cDid ye ever eat dog, Peter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPeter shook his head, disgusted. Not he; nor the Otoes, either. Only\r\nthe northern Indians ate dog.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a t\xe2\x80\x99arin\xe2\x80\x99 lot of \xe2\x80\x99em, anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d mused Patrick Gass. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m after\r\nwishin\xe2\x80\x99 George was here. Sure, he\xe2\x80\x99s like to get into trouble, wanderin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nabout the country where all those fellows are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor two days back George Shannon had been sent out to find the horses\r\nthat had strayed from camp, and he had not returned.\r\n\r\nThe Sioux made a brave sight indeed. They looked to be almost a\r\nhundred\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95ahorse and afoot, with gay streamers and blankets flying.\r\nPierre Dorion and Sergeant Pryor and Private Potts were to be seen,\r\nmounted and riding with the principal chiefs in the advance. So\r\nevidently everything was all right.\r\n\r\nThey halted on the bank opposite the United States camp. Sergeant Pryor\r\nwaved his hat, and the captains sent the red pirogue across for him.\r\nHe and Pierre and Private Potts returned in it. They brought with them\r\nyoung Pierre, who was old Pierre\xe2\x80\x99s son. He was half Sioux, and traded\r\namong the Tetons; but just now he was visiting among the Yanktons.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are friendly, are they, Sergeant?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Captain Lewis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. They treated us very handsomely, and the head chief is\r\nyonder, waiting to talk with you,\xe2\x80\x9d informed Sergeant Pryor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good. You and young Dorion go back to them\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95we\xe2\x80\x99d better send\r\nalong some presents, hadn\xe2\x80\x99t we, Will?\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and tell the chiefs that we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nspeak with them in the morning. \xe2\x80\x99Twon\xe2\x80\x99t do to let them think we\xe2\x80\x99re in\r\nany more of a hurry than they are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Sergeant Pryor.\r\n\r\nHe took over presents of corn and tobacco and iron kettles, with young\r\nPierre to do the translating for him, and returned. Both camps settled\r\ndown for the night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid yez have a rale good time with the Sioux, Nat?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Patrick\r\nGass, that night around the fire, after a hearty supper on cat-fish.\r\nDuring the day a number of huge cat-fish had been caught, some of them\r\nweighing sixty pounds. Now all the men were curious to hear more from\r\nNat Pryor and John Potts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTremendous,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Nat. \xe2\x80\x9cThey wanted to carry us into camp in a\r\nblanket, but we told \xe2\x80\x99em we were not chiefs. They could wait and carry\r\nthe captains. They gave us a fat dog, though, boiled in a pot\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and I\r\nswear he was good eating.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone for me, thank ye,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Sergeant Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 how far is their\r\ncamp, an\xe2\x80\x99 what kind is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s about nine miles back, near the Jacques. All fine buffalo hide\r\nlodges\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95some elk hide, too\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95painted different colors. Fact is, they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nabout the best Indians we\xe2\x80\x99ve met yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe didn\xe2\x80\x99t learn anything of Shannon or the horses, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a word. But I think he\xe2\x80\x99ll be safe if only the Sioux find him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next day dawned so foggy that nobody could see across the river.\r\nThe captains made preparations for the grand council. A pole was set\r\nup, near to a large oak tree, and a new flag hoisted to the top of it.\r\nThe flag was striped red and white; in a corner was a blue square, like\r\nthe sky, studded with stars. \xe2\x80\x99Twas the great flag of the United States\r\nnation\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and Peter thought it beautiful.\r\n\r\nThe two captains dressed in their best. Captain Lewis wore a long coat\r\nof dark blue trimmed with light blue, down its front bright brass\r\nbuttons, and on its shoulders bright gold-fringed epaulets. Captain\r\nClark\xe2\x80\x99s coat was dark blue faced with red; it, too, had the brass\r\nbuttons and the bright epaulets. Both wore their cocked hats, and their\r\nlong knives, or swords.\r\n\r\nThe men also were ordered to put on their best, and to clean up even if\r\nthey had no \xe2\x80\x9cbest.\xe2\x80\x9d Presents were laid out. By the time the fog lifted,\r\nat eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, the camp was ready.\r\n\r\nNow it could be seen that over in the Sioux camp, also, the chiefs and\r\nwarriors were preparing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re painting and polishing, Merne,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Captain Clark, who\r\nhad levelled the spy-glass, to peer.\r\n\r\nThat was so. Peter needed no spy-glass. He could make out figures of\r\nthe chiefs and warriors sitting and plaiting their hair and painting\r\ntheir faces and chests and arms.\r\n\r\nThe two captains waited until nearly noon. Then the red pirogue was\r\ndispatched, under Sergeant Pryor, accompanied by old Pierre, to bring\r\nthe chiefs and warriors. The white pirogue was loaded with goods, but\r\nthe red pirogue had been emptied for repairs. Even then the Sioux so\r\ncrowded it that it scarcely could be rowed. A number of the young Sioux\r\nwaded into the river and swam across.\r\n\r\nNow there were more Sioux than white men in the United States camp.\r\nBut they were armed mainly with bows and arrows, while the United\r\nStates were armed with rifles; and Peter\xe2\x80\x99s sharp eyes observed that the\r\ncannon in the bow of the barge was pointed right at the camp, ready for\r\nbusiness.\r\n\r\nBroad-chested and sinewy were these Yankton Sioux, and evidently great\r\nwarriors. What struck Peter and the soldiers, especially, were the\r\nnecklaces of claws stitched in bands of buckskin or red flannel, and\r\nhanging low on those broad chests. Many warriors wore them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99you mean to say those are b\xe2\x80\x99ar claws!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed John Shields, one\r\nof the Kentuckians.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOui, my frien\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d assured Drouillard, the hunter. \xe2\x80\x9cDey claw of great\r\nwhite bear\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95so we call heem. Beeg! More beeg dan one ox. An\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99fraid? He\r\nnot \xe2\x80\x99fraid of notting. To keel one white bear make Injun beeg warrior.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where do those critters live, then?\xe2\x80\x9d queried John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUp river. We meet \xe2\x80\x99em pret\xe2\x80\x99 queeck, now. Sometime w\xe2\x80\x99en we land\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95woof!\r\nDere coom one beast\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95beeg as one ox\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95mouth he open; an\xe2\x80\x99 mebbe eat us,\r\nif brush so t\xe2\x80\x99ick we not see heem soon \xe2\x80\x99nough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe listening Kentuckians and other soldiers scratched their heads, as\r\nif a little doubtful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith,\xe2\x80\x9d said Patrick Gass, \xe2\x80\x9csome o\xe2\x80\x99 them claws are six inches long,\r\nboys. \xe2\x80\x99Tis a country o\xe2\x80\x99 monsters that we\xe2\x80\x99re goin\xe2\x80\x99 into.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA group of the Sioux had been staring at black York, who, larger than\r\nany of them, was gaping back. Suddenly one stepped to him, wet his\r\nfinger and swiftly drew it down York\xe2\x80\x99s cheek; then looked to see if the\r\nblack had come off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey, you man!\xe2\x80\x9d growled York. \xe2\x80\x9cWha\xe2\x80\x99 foh you done do dat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother Sioux deftly snatched off York\xe2\x80\x99s hat, and clutched the\r\nblack curly wool underneath; but it would not come off, either.\r\nMuch impressed, the circle widened respectfully, and Sioux murmured\r\ngutturally to Sioux.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right, York,\xe2\x80\x9d warned Captain Clark, who had noted; for his\r\nown red hair had been attracting much attention. \xe2\x80\x9cThey say you\xe2\x80\x99re great\r\nmedicine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOui; he black buffalo,\xe2\x80\x9d affirmed young Dorion.\r\n\r\nAfter that York strutted importantly, alarmed the Indians by making\r\nfierce faces, and was followed about by a constant admiring procession.\r\n\r\nThe council was held at noon, under the great oak tree beside which\r\nfloated the United States flag. The chiefs and the leading warriors sat\r\nin a half circle; the two captains sat facing them, Pierre Dorion stood\r\nbefore them as interpreter; and the soldiers and French boatmen sat\r\nbehind in another half circle.\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis made a welcoming speech\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and a fine figure he was,\r\nstanding straight and slim, in his tight-fitting, decorated coat, his\r\ncocked hat with black feather, his sword at his side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe land has changed white fathers,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe great nation of\r\nthe Sioux, and all the other Indians, have a new white father, at\r\nWashington. That is his flag, the flag of the United States nation,\r\nwhich has bought this country. The new father has sent us, who are his\r\nchildren, to tell his red children that he wants them to be at peace\r\nwith one another. I have given flags and peace gifts to the Otoes and\r\nthe Missouris, and have sent word to the Osages and the Omahas and the\r\nPawnees and the Kickapoos and other Indians, that there must be no more\r\nwars among the red children. I will give you a flag and gifts, too, so\r\nthat you will remember what I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the gifts were distributed. To the head chief, Weucha, or Shake\r\nHand, a flag, and a first-grade silver medal, and a paper that\r\ncertified the United States recognized him as the head chief, and\r\na string of beads and shells, and a \xe2\x80\x9cchief\xe2\x80\x99s coat,\xe2\x80\x9d which was a\r\nred-trimmed artillery dress-coat like Captain Clark\xe2\x80\x99s, and a cocked hat\r\nwith red feather in it. Weucha was immensely pleased; he put on the\r\ncoat and hat at once.\r\n\r\nThe four other chiefs also were given gifts. Chief Weucha produced\r\na long peace-pipe of red stone, with reed stem; it was lighted, he\r\npuffed, Captain Lewis and Captain Clark puffed; the four lesser chiefs\r\npuffed. After that the chiefs solemnly shook hands with the captains,\r\nand withdrew into a lean-to of branches, to consult on what they should\r\nreply to-morrow.\r\n\r\nThe Sioux stayed at the camp during the afternoon. The captains gave\r\nthem a dressed deer-hide and an empty keg, for a dance drum. The\r\ndeer-hide was stretched taut over the head of the keg; and that night,\r\nby the light of the fires, the Sioux thumped on the drum and shook\r\ntheir rattles, and danced. One-eyed Cruzatte and George Gibson played\r\non their violins, and the United States warriors danced. But the Sioux\r\nkept it up almost all night, and nobody got much sleep.\r\n\r\nIn the morning after breakfast Weucha and his three sub-chiefs sat\r\nbefore the oak tree; each held a peace pipe in front of him, with the\r\nstem pointing at the spot where the captains were to sit. The names of\r\nthe other chiefs were White Crane, Struck-by-the-Pawnee, and Half Man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ver\xe2\x80\x99 modes\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d explained One-eyed Cruzatte. \xe2\x80\x9cHe say \xe2\x80\x98I am no\r\nwarrior, I only half a man.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWeucha spoke first, standing clad in his artillery coat and cocked\r\nhat. He said that the Yanktons were willing to be at peace, but were\r\nvery poor.\r\n\r\nWhite Crane, and Struck-by-the-Pawnee and Half Man likewise spoke. They\r\nagreed with what Shake Hand had said. They wanted powder and ball, and,\r\ntheir great father\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cmilk\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95which was whisky.\r\n\r\nThat evening the Sioux went back, across the river, well satisfied.\r\nPierre Dorion and young Pierre went with them. Old Pierre promised that\r\nin the spring he would take some of the chiefs to Washington, that they\r\nmight meet their new father.\r\n\r\nJust as the Yanktons were leaving, Captain Lewis beckoned Peter to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better go with Pierre. He will take you down river in the\r\nspring, if not before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, please,\xe2\x80\x9d objected Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cI rather stay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we\xe2\x80\x99re going clear to the Pacific Ocean, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke Captain\r\nClark. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will be a hard trip.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go, too,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cDo not want to stay with Sioux. I am\r\nwhite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you do, along with us, Peter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI work. I can talk sign language,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Peter, proudly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s something in that, Merne,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Captain Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cNow with\r\nDorion gone we\xe2\x80\x99ll need an interpreter to help Drouillard. I fancy Peter\r\nknows almost as much as he does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got a kind heart, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Captain Lewis, his eyes\r\nsoftening. \xe2\x80\x9cBut game\xe2\x80\x99s plenty; we\xe2\x80\x99ll have meat enough\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and that\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nmain question. All right, Peter. You can come as far as the Mandan\r\nvillage, anyway. And in the spring we\xe2\x80\x99ll see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhereupon Peter resolved that he would make himself useful, so that\r\nthey would take him clear to the Pacific Ocean, which lay, according to\r\nPatrick Gass and the other men, many, many days\xe2\x80\x99 travel, far beyond the\r\nwestern mountains.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nV\r\n\r\nBAD HEARTS\r\n\r\n\r\nWork, work, work! Through this the month of September, 1804, the boats\r\nhad been toiling on up the sluggish Missouri River, in the present\r\nState of South Dakota. With the rains, the winds, and the shallows,\r\neverybody, even the captains, was wet all the day, from hauling on the\r\ntow-ropes, in and out of the water.\r\n\r\nThe weather turned cold and raw. Shelters of deer hides were stretched\r\nover the two pirogues, and in the camps the men made themselves hide\r\ncoats and leggins and moccasins. Patrick and old Cruzatte together\r\nfitted Peter with a buckskin suit that felt much better to him than his\r\nother, clumsy garments.\r\n\r\nAfter having been gone over two weeks, George Shannon appeared at last,\r\nriding through the rain, with only one horse. He had been lost, and had\r\nalmost starved, and the other horse had broken down. All were glad to\r\nsee George again.\r\n\r\nBut where, now, were the Teton Sioux? George reported that he had seen\r\nnone.\r\n\r\nThe last week in September a great smoke was sighted in the distance;\r\nand that night three Indian boys swam the river, to enter the camp.\r\nThey were Tetons, from two villages a few miles above.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive them some tobacco,\xe2\x80\x9d directed Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cTell them to say to\r\ntheir chiefs that we will hold a council to-morrow morning, near the\r\nvillages.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the way up, Reuben Fields, who had been hunting, horseback, returned\r\nafoot and signalled to be taken aboard. He said that some Indians had\r\nstolen his horse while he was dressing an elk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOui,\xe2\x80\x9d chirped Drouillard. \xe2\x80\x9cDose Tetons haf bad hearts. We best look\r\nsharp or dey take scalps, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe mustn\xe2\x80\x99t let them have the idea they can plunder us,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke Captain\r\nLewis, reddening. \xe2\x80\x9cThis leaves us without horses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t those several Indians, on the bank ahead?\xe2\x80\x9d presently queried\r\nCaptain Clark.\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis peered through his spy-glass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive of them. We\xe2\x80\x99ll stop and hail them, and hear what they have to\r\nsay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think they\xe2\x80\x99re the fellows who stole your horse, Fields?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nCaptain Clark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Reuben. \xe2\x80\x9cI had only a glimpse of the\r\nthieves, and these Injuns mainly look alike, sir, till you get to know\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe five Indians on the bank stolidly waited, while the barge hove to,\r\nopposite.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre they Tetons, Drouillard?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Captain Lewis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOui,\xe2\x80\x9d nodded Drouillard. \xe2\x80\x9cDey Tetons. Eh, Cruzatte?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMais, oui,\xe2\x80\x9d confirmed One-eyed Cruzatte. \xe2\x80\x9cBeeg rascals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Tell them that some of their young men have stolen a horse\r\nfrom their great father at Washington, and we want it returned or we\r\nwill hold no council. We\xe2\x80\x99re willing to be friends, but we aren\xe2\x80\x99t afraid\r\nof them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know much of dees Sioux tongue, but I will try,\xe2\x80\x9d engaged\r\nDrouillard. And by signs and a few words he delivered the message.\r\n\r\nThe Indians consulted a moment together; then one of them replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI t\xe2\x80\x99ink dey say dey haf not seen a hoss,\xe2\x80\x9d translated Drouillard. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nif it is found it will be return\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI t\xe2\x80\x99ink so, too,\xe2\x80\x9d added the funny Cruzatte\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95although everybody was\r\naware that he did not understand a word of Sioux.\r\n\r\nHowever, by the signs that were made, Peter would have interpreted\r\nthe same as Drouillard. He and the Oto boys had practiced for hours,\r\ntalking sign language.\r\n\r\nThe boats stopped for the night off the mouth of a river on the left\r\nor the south. This night only a few men were allowed ashore, to guard\r\nthe cook fires; the remainder slept aboard the boats, with their guns\r\nready. The captains named the river Teton River, but it was soon\r\nrenamed Bad River, for very good reason.\r\n\r\nIn the morning everybody, except the boat guards, landed. The\r\ncaptains ordered the United States flag hoisted, again, on a pole,\r\nand the awning was stretched, as at the camp where the Otoes had been\r\nentertained. All the soldiers ashore were formed in rank, under arms,\r\nfacing the flag-pole and the canopy; and soon the Tetons came in to\r\ncouncil, from their village two miles up-river.\r\n\r\nThere were about sixty of them. They were not nearly so good-looking as\r\nthe Yanktons, being smaller, with slim crooked legs and lean arms, and\r\neyes set over high cheek-bones.\r\n\r\nThe council did not pass off very satisfactorily, because Drouillard\r\nknew little Teton talk, and scarcely could make himself understood when\r\nhe talked for Captain Lewis. Still, the head chief, Black Buffalo,\r\nwas given a medal, and a United States flag, and a red coat decorated\r\nwith white lace, and a cocked hat with red feather. The second chief,\r\nTor-to-hon-ga or Partisan, and the third chief, Buffalo Medicine, were\r\ngiven medals and beads and tobacco. Two warriors, Wah-zing-go, and\r\nMat-o-co-que-pa or Second Bear, also were rewarded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you suppose those raven scalps signify?\xe2\x80\x9d asked George Shannon.\r\nFor the two warriors wore each two or three raven skins fastened to\r\ntheir waists behind, with the tails sticking out, and on their heads\r\nwas another raven skin, flattened with the beak to the fore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDey special soldier,\xe2\x80\x9d explained old Cruzatte. \xe2\x80\x9cW\xe2\x80\x99at you call\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95marshal.\r\nOui. Dey boss. Obey nobody but chief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the captains took them all aboard the barge to show them the\r\ncannon and the air-gun that shot forty times, and other wonders.\r\nCaptain Clark brought them ashore again in the red pirogue.\r\n\r\nNo sooner had the cable been carried on shore, to be held by Patrick\r\nGass and Reuben Fields and George Shannon while the load was landed,\r\nand Captain Clark had stepped out, than three of the Indians grabbed\r\nit, and Wah-zing-go, the warrior, put his arms about the mast, as if to\r\nkeep the boat there. Tor-to-hon-ga began to talk in a loud and angry\r\nvoice. Captain Clark flushed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does he say, Peter?\xe2\x80\x9d he appealed. For Drouillard was on the\r\nbarge, and only Peter was near. When the five men had started to row\r\nthe pirogue ashore, with the chiefs and Captain Clark, he had slipped\r\nin, too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe chief say you cannot go away till you give them more presents,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ntranslated Peter, boldly; for he had picked up some Sioux words and he\r\ncould read the gestures, also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d And Captain Clark was angry indeed. He had only five men, two\r\nin the boat and three ashore, but he was not afraid. \xe2\x80\x9cYou tell him we\r\nwill go on, and he can\xe2\x80\x99t stop us. We are not squaws, but warriors. Our\r\ngreat father has medicine on those boats that will wipe out twenty\r\nSioux nations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe chief says he has plenty warriors, too,\xe2\x80\x9d interpreted Peter.\r\n\r\nAnd at that moment the chief sprang for Captain Clark; the warriors\r\nspread right and left, jerked arrows from quivers and fitted them to\r\nstrung bows. Out whipped Captain Clark\xe2\x80\x99s bright sword\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95the long knife;\r\nand Chief Tor-to-hon-ga dodged. Captain Clark\xe2\x80\x99s face was redder than\r\nhis hair. He acted like a great chief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWatch out, Sergeant!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, to Patrick Gass. \xe2\x80\x9cRally on the boat;\r\nnever mind the rope. Face them and stand together, men!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis\xe2\x80\x99s voice rang high and stern, from the barge. Out of the\r\nwhite pirogue a dozen men plashed into the shallows and wading and\r\nplunging, hastened to reinforce the red pirogue. Corporal Warfington\r\nand the six St. Louis soldiers who had been sent along to help as far\r\nas the Mandans were with them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteady!\xe2\x80\x9d warned Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cLook sharp, Will.\xe2\x80\x9d And now the black\r\nmuzzle of the cannon in the bows of the barge swung full at the shore.\r\nBehind it stood Gunner Alexander Willard, with lighted match.\r\n\r\nThis was enough. Head Chief Black Buffalo shouted an order, and his men\r\nleft the cable and the pirogue and fell back. The \xe2\x80\x9cmedicine\xe2\x80\x9d of the\r\ngreat father at Washington was, they realized, strong medicine.\r\n\r\nTo show that he was not afraid, and that he wished to be friendly,\r\nCaptain Clark offered to shake hands with Black Buffalo and Partisan;\r\nbut they surlily refused. So the captain laughed, and ordered the red\r\npirogue to return to the barge. Then Black Buffalo and Partisan, and\r\nthe warriors Wah-zing-go and Second Bear ran after, through the water,\r\nand climbed aboard, to go on the barge also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather a close shave, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cAn instant more\r\nand I\xe2\x80\x99d have helped you out with a round of grape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey wished to try our metal,\xe2\x80\x9d smiled Captain Clark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were afraid the white chiefs would go on and not stop at our\r\nvillage to show our squaws and boys the great father\xe2\x80\x99s boats,\xe2\x80\x9d alleged\r\nChief Black Buffalo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him we are willing to be friends, and will stop,\xe2\x80\x9d directed\r\nCaptain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cThe soldiers of the great father do not fear the Sioux.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf head chief he not tell dat raven soldier to let go mast, he hang on\r\ntill cut in leetle pieces,\xe2\x80\x9d was saying Cruzatte.\r\n\r\nIn the morning the boats were moved up to the village, and Captain\r\nLewis went ashore. Truly, the Red Head and the slim Captain Lewis were\r\nbrave men. Peter was proud to have been by Captain Clark\xe2\x80\x99s side, in the\r\nfracas. It was fine to be a United States.\r\n\r\nWhen Captain Lewis returned on board, he told Captain Clark that\r\neverything was all right, and that the Tetons were waiting for the Red\r\nHead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a bigger man than I am, Will, after the stand you made\r\nyesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d he laughed.\r\n\r\nAnd it seemed to be that way, for when Captain Clark landed he was\r\nmet by ten young warriors, with a gaily decorated buffalo robe. They\r\ncarried him upon it, and then bore him, sitting in it, to the council\r\nhouse. This was great honor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re nixt, Cap\xe2\x80\x99n,\xe2\x80\x9d ventured Patrick Gass. \xe2\x80\x9cThere they are, back for\r\nye, sorr.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe alert, Sergeant,\xe2\x80\x9d bade the captain, as he vaulted from the barge\r\ninto the pirogue. \xe2\x80\x9cThey may appear friendly, but we mustn\xe2\x80\x99t take any\r\nchances. Don\xe2\x80\x99t let the men lay aside their arms for a minute, and keep\r\nthem together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYis, sorr. I will, sorr,\xe2\x80\x9d promised Patrick Gass. He was the oldest\r\nsoldier in the company, and the captains relied upon him.\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis likewise was borne to the council house; and the men of\r\nthe expedition, except the boat guards, marched after.\r\n\r\nThe council lasted a long time, and was concluded with a feast of the\r\ndog-meat from a pot, and of buffalo meat and hominy and ground-potato.\r\nBuffalo meat was given to the white chiefs as a present. The Tetons\r\nclaimed to be poor, but they weren\xe2\x80\x99t. This was a powerful and rich\r\nvillage, as anybody might see. Before the dance that had been planned\r\nfor the evening, the men were permitted to roam about a little. Peter\r\nand Patrick Gass and their party discovered a string of scalps hanging\r\nfrom a pole, and a number of Omaha squaws and children who appeared\r\nvery miserable.\r\n\r\nPeter talked with them a little. They were prisoners. The Tetons had\r\nattacked their village down the river, and had burned forty lodges and\r\nkilled seventy-five warriors.\r\n\r\nWhen dusk fell the dance was started, by the light of a fire, in\r\nthe middle of the council house. The Sioux warriors danced, and the\r\nSioux women danced; but at midnight the captains told the chief that\r\neverybody was tired and it was time to go to bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe chief he say: \xe2\x80\x98Ver\xe2\x80\x99 well. Now sleep. To-morrow more Sioux come,\r\nto talk with de great father.\xe2\x80\x99 He want you to stay,\xe2\x80\x9d interpreted\r\nDrouillard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will stay and see these other Sioux,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\ndo you think, Will?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you say so, Merne,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Captain Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there\xe2\x80\x99s some trick\r\nin this. We mustn\xe2\x80\x99t be caught off guard\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and of course we mustn\xe2\x80\x99t show\r\nthat we\xe2\x80\x99re afraid, either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut no visiting Sioux turned up, although the boats waited all day. At\r\nnight another dance was given.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe in bad feex,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted One-eyed Cruzatte. \xe2\x80\x9cDose Teton, dey keep us.\r\nI t\xe2\x80\x99ink dey plan mischief. I wish we go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverybody was nervous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I wonder if we\xe2\x80\x99re in for a fight,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke Corporal Warfington.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Patrick Gass, \xe2\x80\x9cwe can lick \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAmidst the dusk ashore, while Peter, tired of the noise and dancing,\r\nwas wandering a few steps, a low voice hailed him, in Oto.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHist! You Oto?\xe2\x80\x9d It was one of the Omaha squaws. How could she have\r\nguessed that he had been an Oto?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. White,\xe2\x80\x9d responded Peter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell your chiefs the Sioux are bad. They will not let the big boats\r\ngo. They play you a trick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell,\xe2\x80\x9d responded Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cYou speak Oto well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Omaha, but I was in Oto village once. I saw you.\xe2\x80\x9d And the squaw\r\nvanished.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVI\r\n\r\nTHE CAPTAINS SHOW THEIR SPUNK\r\n\r\n\r\nPeter believed that the Omaha woman spoke the truth. The captains ought\r\nto be told at once. But the dancing was still in progress in the lodge\r\nof Chief Black Buffalo, where sat the two captains and the chiefs,\r\nwatching. A boy would not be admitted. So Peter sought out Sergeant\r\nJohn Ordway, who was in charge of the shore guard. John Ordway was\r\nnot from Kentucky; he was from a place called New Hampshire, in the\r\nnortheast of the United States.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t say!\xe2\x80\x9d replied John Ordway, when Peter had told him of the\r\nwarning from the Omaha woman. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, anybody might suspect as much.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll get word to the captains, first chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe dancing continued until late, again. Peter curled in the bows of\r\nthe waiting pirogue, and went to sleep. He had done his duty and could\r\ntrust to John Ordway. By the stars it was midnight when he awakened at\r\nthe approach of the captains. They and two Indian guests and the guard\r\nclambered in, and the pirogue was rowed for the barge.\r\n\r\nThe shore was silent and dark\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95but how alert were those Sioux! The\r\npirogue ran against the anchor cable of the barge, in the darkness, and\r\nbroke it. The barge was adrift. The captains cried loudly, ordering\r\nthe oars to be manned and the barge held until a cable could be passed\r\nashore\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and instantly the two Indians in the pirogue shouted excitedly,\r\nin the Sioux tongue, summoning the village.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere! Quick!\xe2\x80\x9d they called. \xe2\x80\x9cTo the boats! Come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe whole village burst into an uproar; the warriors poured forth to\r\nthe water\xe2\x80\x99s edge. It was very plain that they feared the white men were\r\nleaving. The captains could pay little attention until a cable had been\r\ncarried from the barge and fastened to a tree on the bank, and the\r\nbarge pulled in out of the current. Then\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk Tor-to-hon-ga what\xe2\x80\x99s the meaning of all this alarm,\xe2\x80\x9d bade Captain\r\nLewis, tersely, of Drouillard. Tor-to-hon-ga was one of the two guests.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe say de Tetons \xe2\x80\x99fraid de \xe2\x80\x99Maha warriors haf come up an\xe2\x80\x99 attack de\r\nboats of de great white father,\xe2\x80\x9d interpreted Drouillard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Captain Lewis.\r\n\r\nAnd anybody might see how foolish was this excuse of the Tetons: that\r\nthe Omahas would attack boats defended by guns, when the Sioux were the\r\nreal enemies. After the village was quiet again, at least sixty Teton\r\nwarriors remained there on the bank, all night, ready for action.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI t\xe2\x80\x99ink,\xe2\x80\x9d commented Drouillard, \xe2\x80\x9cmebbe we have leetle trouble, in\r\nmornin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re in a bad box,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Sergeant Ordway. \xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99re tied up close\r\nto the bank, under direct fire. We may have a hard time casting off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStrong guards were kept under arms, on all the boats. There was\r\nlittle sleep. Both captains were constantly about, peering through\r\nthe darkness, and listening. Early in the morning the Tetons were\r\nassembled; and while Patrick Gass and a detail were dragging from a\r\npirogue, trying to find the barge\xe2\x80\x99s anchor, several chiefs and warriors\r\nwaded out to the barge and climbed aboard.\r\n\r\nThe anchor could not be found.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll go on without it. Send those\r\nfellows ashore, Will. Sergeant Pryor, take a squad with you and cast\r\noff that rope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Indian visitors did not wish to go ashore, but Captain Clark\r\nordered them pushed into the pirogue which was to bear Sergeant Pryor\r\nand squad. Chief Black Buffalo still refused to go. Sergeant Pryor\r\nreleased the rope from the tree on the bank and returned. The sail on\r\nthe barge was being hoisted\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and at the instant laughter and shouts\r\nmingled, both ashore and from the boats.\r\n\r\nA number of the Sioux had sat upon the rope, holding it!\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis flared into hot rage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake charge of the pirogues, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d he ordered. \xe2\x80\x9cDown behind the\r\ngunwale, men. Advance your rifles. See that the priming\xe2\x80\x99s fresh,\r\nOrdway and Gass. Stand to your swivel, Willard!\xe2\x80\x9d And, to Chief Black\r\nBuffalo: \xe2\x80\x9cMy young men are ready for battle. If your young men do not\r\nrelease the rope we will fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe say de young men want leetle more tobac\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d translated Drouillard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him we have given all the presents that we\xe2\x80\x99re going to give,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncrisply answered Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95wait. Here!\xe2\x80\x9d And snatching a roll\r\nof tobacco, Captain Lewis threw it at Black Buffalo\xe2\x80\x99s feet. \xe2\x80\x9cTell him\r\nthere is his tobacco, on the prairie. He says he is a great chief.\r\nAmong the white men great chiefs are obeyed. If he is a great chief let\r\nhim order his young men to release that rope and they will obey him.\r\nBut we do not believe he is a great chief. He is a squaw, and the young\r\nmen laugh at him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWah!\xe2\x80\x9d grunted Chief Black Buffalo, when he heard. He seized the\r\ntobacco and leaped from the boat, to surge for the shore. There he\r\ntumbled his young men right and left, snatched the rope and hurled it\r\nout into the water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo,\xe2\x80\x9d he bawled. Thus he proved himself to be the great chief.\r\n\r\nThe soldiers cheered. The barge\xe2\x80\x99s sail caught the breeze, the barge\r\nmoved. Just in time Captain Clark leaped from the pirogue, into which\r\nhe had transferred, and gained the gunwale, and the deck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell done, Merne,\xe2\x80\x9d he panted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGolly!\xe2\x80\x9d babbled York. \xe2\x80\x9cDat chief mighty brash when he get started.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe barge and the pirogues gained the middle of the river. Rapidly the\r\nTeton village was left behind. Patrick Gass waved his hat derisively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBad luck to yez,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cSure, an\xe2\x80\x99 if we\xe2\x80\x99d stayed a minute longer\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99d ha\xe2\x80\x99 put your town into mournin\xe2\x80\x99. We\xe2\x80\x99re not so paceful as we\r\nlook.\xe2\x80\x9d And he added: \xe2\x80\x9cThe \xe2\x80\x99Rikaras nixt. We\xe2\x80\x99ll hope they be gintlemen.\r\nAnnyhow, we\xe2\x80\x99ve no horses left for \xe2\x80\x99em to stale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJust what was to be expected from the Arikaras nobody might say, but\r\nalthough they were warlike they were thought to be not so mean as the\r\nTeton Sioux. The boats forged on, and the month changed to that of\r\nOctober.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow far to the \xe2\x80\x99Rikara villages, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Captain Lewis, of a\r\ntrader named Valle who came aboard the barge for a talk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy river about 100 miles, captain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFrom an excursion ashore with Captain Clark and squad, York returned\r\ntremendously excited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe done found one o\xe2\x80\x99 dem white b\xe2\x80\x99ars,\xe2\x80\x9d proclaimed York. \xe2\x80\x9cYessuh, me\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 Marse Will. Oof!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99bouts, York?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhar\xe2\x80\x99s his scalp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you get a shot at him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nQuestions were volleyed thick and fast. York wagged his woolly head and\r\nrolled his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNossuh. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t get no shot at him. We des seen his track, in dem\r\nbushes yonduh near de mout\xe2\x80\x99 ob de ribber. Oof! Marse Will he set his\r\nmoccasin cl\xe2\x80\x99ar inside, an\xe2\x80\x99 dat track it stuck out all \xe2\x80\x99round. \xe2\x80\x99Spec\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndis chile ain\xe2\x80\x99t got bus\xe2\x80\x99ness wif dem critters. Oof!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed George Shannon. \xe2\x80\x9cAccording to Drouillard even the Indians\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t tackle one of those white bears, except in a crowd of six or\r\neight. And if they don\xe2\x80\x99t shoot him through the head or heart he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nliable to out-fight them all. Before they go after him they make big\r\nmedicine, same as if they were going to war with a whole nation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x99special fond of black meat, too, I hear tell,\xe2\x80\x9d slyly remarked\r\nJohn Thompson.\r\n\r\nYork rolled his eyes, and muttered. But the Kentuckians, some of\r\nwhom had hunted with Daniel Boone, fingered their rifles eagerly and\r\nsurveyed the low country at the mouth of the river, as if hoping to see\r\nYork\xe2\x80\x99s monster stirring.\r\n\r\nThe next day the first Arikara Indians came aboard, from their lower\r\nvillage. Captain Lewis went with some of them to return the visit.\r\nHe was accompanied back by Mr. Tabeau and Mr. Gravelines, two French\r\ntraders who lived with the Arikaras. Mr. Gravelines spoke the Arikara\r\nlanguage.\r\n\r\nThere were three Arikara villages, so that the captains ordered camp\r\nmade on the north side of the river, across from the villages.\r\n\r\nThe Arikaras were tall, handsome people\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95much superior, thought\r\nPatrick Gass and the rest of the men, to the Sioux. Chiefs\r\nKa-ka-wis-sas-sa or Lighting Crow, Fo-cas-se or Hay, and Pi-a-he-to or\r\nEagle\xe2\x80\x99s Feather, were introduced by Mr. Gravelines, and the camp soon\r\nfilled with the Arikara warriors, and even squaws who rowed across in\r\nlittle skin boats of a single buffalo hide stretched over basket-work.\r\n\r\nYork held a regular reception, for he appeared to astonish the Arikaras\r\nas much as he had astonished the Sioux.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey, Marse Tabeau,\xe2\x80\x9d he called, to the French trader. \xe2\x80\x9cDes tell dese\r\npeople I\xe2\x80\x99se bohn wil\xe2\x80\x99, an\xe2\x80\x99 my young marster done ketched me when I was\r\nrunnin\xe2\x80\x99 in de timber an\xe2\x80\x99 tamed me. Tell \xe2\x80\x99em I used to eat peoples bones\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 all. I\xe2\x80\x99se a sorter g\xe2\x80\x99riller.\xe2\x80\x9d And thereupon York seized a thick\r\nstick, and snapped it in his two hands, and howled and gritted his\r\nteeth. He was very strong, was York.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh!\xe2\x80\x9d grunted the Arikaras, respectfully falling back from him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will do, York,\xe2\x80\x9d cautioned Captain Clark, trying not to laugh.\r\n\r\nBut York, of much importance, thoroughly enjoyed himself.\r\n\r\nThe Arikaras were splendid entertainers and exceedingly hospitable\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Mos\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlike white folks,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted York. They did not beg, as the Sioux had\r\nbegged; they gave lavishly out of their store of corn and beans and dried\r\nsquashes, and accepted thankfully the gifts from the great father; they\r\nwould not drink any whisky\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x9cWe are surprised that the great father\r\nshould send us liquor to make fools of us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chief Lighting Crow.\r\nTheir houses were built close together, of a willow frame plastered with\r\nmud, and were entered through a covered passage-way that kept out the\r\nwind. Around each village was a fence of close upright pickets, for\r\ndefense. They were well armed, too, with guns.\r\n\r\nWhen it came time, after the councils had been held, to leave the\r\nfriendly Arikaras, all the men of the expedition hated to go. John\r\nNewman, who had enlisted at St. Louis, was the most out-spoken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here,\xe2\x80\x9d he uttered, boldly, among his comrades at the last camp\r\nfire. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should we go on, up to those Mandans? Why can\xe2\x80\x99t we spend the\r\nwinter where we are? The Mandan village is nigh on 200 miles yet, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m tired of working my hands raw in this cold weather, hauling the\r\nboats over sand-bars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOrders be orders,\xe2\x80\x9d reminded Patrick Gass. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 up to the Mandans we\r\ngo, I\xe2\x80\x99m thinkin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if we show a little spunk and say we want to stay,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhisht, now!\xe2\x80\x9d cautioned Patrick. \xe2\x80\x9cWould ye spoil a good record?\r\nFaith,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cif the captain heard ye he\xe2\x80\x99ll have ye on the carpet\r\nfor mutiny, b\xe2\x80\x99gorry.\xe2\x80\x9d Captain Clark had strode hastily by, wrapped in\r\nhis cloak. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s mutiny ye\xe2\x80\x99re talkin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d scolded Patrick Gass. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nwant no more of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Clark had heard, for at breaking camp in the morning, John was\r\nplaced under arrest and confined in the forecastle aboard the barge.\r\n\r\nThat night, at camp, twenty-five miles above the Arikara villages, a\r\ncourt-martial was held on the case of John Newman. He was found guilty\r\nof mutinous speech and sentenced to received seventy-five lashes, and\r\nbe suspended from the company. The next noon the boats stopped in the\r\nrain, at a sand-bar in the middle of the river, everybody was ordered\r\nout, and John was roundly whipped on the naked back with ramrods and\r\nswitches.\r\n\r\nChief Ah-ke-tah-na-sha of the Arikaras, who was going with the\r\nexpedition up to the Mandans, to make peace between the Mandans and\r\nthe Arikaras, squatted on the sand-bar, to watch. Evidently he did not\r\nunderstand, for he began to weep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy does Ah-ke-tah-na-sha cry?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Captain Clark.\r\n\r\nAh-ke-tah-na-sha, who could speak some Sioux, explained to Drouillard,\r\nand Drouillard explained to the captains.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe say de \xe2\x80\x99Rikara dey punish by death, but dey never whip even de\r\nchildren. He weep for Newman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him what the matter is, and that this is the white man\xe2\x80\x99s way of\r\npunishing disobedience,\xe2\x80\x9d directed Captain Clark, to Drouillard.\r\n\r\nDrouillard did; and reported.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe say mebbe so, but \xe2\x80\x99mong Injuns to whip men make women of dem. If\r\ndees is white man way, all right. Men ought to obey deir chiefs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow aren\xe2\x80\x99t ye \xe2\x80\x99shamed o\xe2\x80\x99 yourself, when even an Injun cries over ye?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nreproved Patrick Gass, of John Newman, who was painfully donning his\r\nshirt and coat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted John. \xe2\x80\x9cI guess I deserved what I got. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nharbor any grudge, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do my duty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\nSNUG IN WINTER QUARTERS\r\n\r\n\r\nThe weather had grown much colder, with squalls of snow and sleet and\r\nhigh winds; the wild geese were flying high, headed into the south; and\r\nthe river, falling rapidly, was split with bars and narrow channels,\r\nwhen, two weeks after the punishment of John Newman, the barge and the\r\ntwo pirogues anchored off the first of the Mandan villages, in the\r\ncentre of present North Dakota.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive long months we\xe2\x80\x99ve been travelin\xe2\x80\x99, an\xe2\x80\x99 for sixteen hundred crooked\r\nmiles,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Patrick Gass. \xe2\x80\x9cSure we desarve a bit o\xe2\x80\x99 rist. Now what\r\nwill the Mandans say, I wonder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see that young fellow who\xe2\x80\x99d lost the halves of two fingers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nqueried George Shannon. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, he\xe2\x80\x99d cut \xe2\x80\x99em off, on purpose, because\r\nsome of his relatives had died! That\xe2\x80\x99s the Mandan way of going into\r\nmourning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twould be better to cut the hair, I\xe2\x80\x99m thinkin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cThey most\r\nof \xe2\x80\x99em nade it\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95an\xe2\x80\x99 hair\xe2\x80\x99ll grow again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Mandans had swarmed aboard, and were examining every object\r\nwith much curiosity. They were an odd people, wrinkled and of low\r\nstature\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95many of the women with brown hair, but others with gray hair\r\nwhich flared almost to the ground. However, their voices were gentle,\r\nand they brought gifts of corn and vegetables, in earthen jars.\r\n\r\nMr. Jessaume, a French trader among them, also came aboard; so did a\r\nScotchman named Hugh McCracken, from a British fur company post far\r\nnorth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re frindly, be they, Pierre?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Pat, of One-eyed Cruzatte,\r\nwho was hobbling past after a lively conversation with Mr. Jessaume.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOui,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Cruzatte, with a grimace of pain. \xe2\x80\x9cI t\xe2\x80\x99ink we stay an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nspen\xe2\x80\x99 one winter. Dey glad. We protect\xe2\x80\x99 dem \xe2\x80\x99gainst de Sioux. My poor\r\nleg, he carry me not furder, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor Cruzatte had the rheumatism in both knees. Reuben Fields was laid\r\nup with the rheumatism in his neck; and Captain Clark had been so\r\nbothered with a stiff neck that he could not move around until Captain\r\nLewis had applied a hot stone wrapped in red flannel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHi!\xe2\x80\x9d cackled big York, strutting as usual. \xe2\x80\x9cDese heah Mandans done gif\r\nme name Great Medicine, Mistuh McCracken say.
