But it\r\nrained almost every day, and every morning a fog hung low.\r\n\r\nOn the morning of November 7 the camp rose and breakfasted in a wet\r\nmist so dense that it hung on all sides like a gray curtain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt this rate,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Pat, as the canoes headed out into the silence,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99re liable to get half way to Chiny afore we know we\xe2\x80\x99re on the\r\nPaycific at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do believe I smell salt, though,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted George Shannon,\r\nsniffing. \xe2\x80\x9cSa-ca-ja-we-a\xe2\x80\x99s been insisting, too, that she could hear a\r\n\xe2\x80\x98boom-boom.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen!\xe2\x80\x9d bade Pat\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and they paused on their oars. Peter thought that\r\nhe also could hear a \xe2\x80\x9cboom-boom,\xe2\x80\x9d low and dull, but he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t certain.\r\nThey went on.\r\n\r\nThe captains\xe2\x80\x99 boat was being piloted by a Wah-kia-cum Indian, now: a\r\nsquat ugly man who wore a queer round jacket that, according to the\r\nmen, had come from a ship. The river was growing wider, the fog was\r\nthinning and lifting\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95on a sudden the crew of the captains\xe2\x80\x99 boat waved\r\ntheir hats, pointed before, cheered wildly. The cheer passed from boat\r\nto boat. For the fog ahead had swirled into fragments, and below it was\r\nan expanse of tumbling gray water on which the sun was trying to shine.\r\nOccasionally sounded a muffled \xe2\x80\x9cboom,\xe2\x80\x9d like the faint growl of summer\r\nthunder.\r\n\r\nThe Pacific Ocean! But they did not reach it this day; the fog closed\r\nin again, and the rain. They did not reach it the next day, although\r\nthe waves were so high in this, the mouth of the Columbia, that half\r\nthe party were seasick; and the water was salty. They did not reach\r\nit the next day, nor the next. Wind and rain kept beating them back.\r\nSa-ca-ja-we-a was frightened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe spirits are angry. They do not want us here,\xe2\x80\x9d she whimpered,\r\ncrouching over little Toussaint, under a grass mat raised on a pole.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe only way we\xe2\x80\x99ll reach the sea is to be washed into it,\xe2\x80\x9d groaned\r\nPat. \xe2\x80\x9cSure, don\xe2\x80\x99t the very stones an\xe2\x80\x99 logs come a-rollin\xe2\x80\x99 down the\r\nhills? Now for the first time I wish I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t started, an\xe2\x80\x99 here I am at\r\nthe ind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYes, miserable were they all. There was no chance to dry clothing and\r\nfood, and scarcely an opportunity to stir. The mouth of the river\r\nformed a wind-swept bay miles wide. The captains thought that if camp\r\nmight only be moved around a point ahead, and to a high sand beach,\r\nit would be more comfortable. A deserted Indian village stood there,\r\nwith no inhabitants \xe2\x80\x9cexcept fleas\xe2\x80\x9d; and, as Pat said: \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll be all the\r\nwarmer for the exercise they give us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNot until the afternoon of November 15 did the opportunity to move\r\ncome. The sky cleared, the wind suddenly dropped; the canoes were\r\nreloaded in a hurry, and the point was rounded.\r\n\r\nNow the ocean was in full sight, outside the bay; from the boards of\r\nthe Indian houses rude cabins were erected; hunters and explorers were\r\nsent out.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVI\r\n\r\nTHE WINTER AT FORT CLATSOP\r\n\r\n\r\nBut no ships from the United States or any other nation were to be\r\nfound. Only the long gray swells appeared, as far as eye could see,\r\nrolling in to burst thunderously upon the white sands and the naked\r\nrocks; and the only people ashore were the Indians. Ships and white men\r\nhad been here, said the Indians, during the summer; and many of the\r\nIndians spoke a curious mixture of English and native words. Captain\r\nLewis discovered a place, in the bay, where white men had camped.\r\n\r\nA high point overlooking the lonely ocean was given the name Cape\r\nDisappointment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it have been a fine end to our trip from the Mississippi\r\nclane to the Paycific if a nice big ship all stocked with flour an\xe2\x80\x99\r\np\xe2\x80\x99taties an\xe2\x80\x99 boots an\xe2\x80\x99 socks had been waitin\xe2\x80\x99 for us,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Pat.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, mebbe the United States has forgotten us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have to build winter quarters at once, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain\r\nLewis. \xe2\x80\x9cThe rain is rotting all our goods and clothes, and spoiling our\r\nprovisions. We must get under cover. There\xe2\x80\x99ll be no ships before next\r\nsummer, according to the Indians.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTimber for cabins, wood for fires, game and fresh water for the\r\nmesses, and shelter from the ocean tides\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95let\xe2\x80\x99s look about, then,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nanswered Captain Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Indians say that skins and meat are\r\nabundant a little way south.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis found it\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95a good site, on the south side of the bay\r\nformed by the mouth of the Columbia, and three miles up a little river\r\ncalled to-day the Lewis and Clark River. It was back ten miles from the\r\nocean, and in the midst of tall pines, with great shaking bogs near, on\r\nwhich elk fed.\r\n\r\nThe first fair morning, which was December 7, camp was moved to the new\r\ngrounds.\r\n\r\nThe walls of the seven cabins rose fast; and when it came time to put\r\non the roofs, Pat, the boss carpenter, was delighted to find a species\r\nof pine that split into boards ten feet long, and two feet wide, with\r\nnever a knot or crack.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe finest puncheons I iver have seen,\xe2\x80\x9d he asserted, \xe2\x80\x9cfor floors\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 roofs both. We\xe2\x80\x99ll be snug an\xe2\x80\x99 dry in a jiffy, an\xe2\x80\x99 all ready for\r\nChristmas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a far cry back to last Christmas, Pat,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke George. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve come\r\nthrough a lot of country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 here we are,\xe2\x80\x9d reminded Pat.\r\n\r\nYes; Christmas\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95Peter\xe2\x80\x99s first Christmas\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95was indeed a long way behind.\r\nThat Christmas of 1804 had been celebrated in new Fort Mandan among\r\nthe Mandans and Minnetarees beside the snowy Missouri River. What were\r\nChiefs Big White and Black Cat doing now? Was Fort Mandan being kept\r\nready for the return of the Long Knife and the Red Head?\r\n\r\nThis Christmas of 1805 was celebrated in new Fort Clatsop, among the\r\nflat-headed Clatsops and Chinooks and Cathlamets at the mouth of the\r\nrainy Columbia River. The men fired a volley, before breakfast, and in\r\nfront of the captains\xe2\x80\x99 door old Cruzatte, accompanied by Drouillard and\r\nthe other Frenchmen, sang a lively Christmas song. But there was no\r\nfeast, because the only food in stock was some roots, pounded fish, and\r\nlean elk meat. The captains distributed a little tobacco to the men who\r\nsmoked, and Peter and the men who did not use tobacco received each a\r\nhandkerchief.\r\n\r\nThe rain poured all day, but the cabins were tight above and below, so\r\nthat everybody stayed dry and warm.\r\n\r\nNow the expedition might settle down to the winter\xe2\x80\x99s routine. Chimneys\r\nwere yet to be put up for the men\xe2\x80\x99s cabins\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95fires were tried, in open\r\nhearths in the middle of the rooms, Indian fashion, and proved too\r\nsmoky. A fence of high, close pickets, as at Fort Mandan, needs must be\r\nerected to guard against attack.\r\n\r\nThe captains\xe2\x80\x99 cabin had been built around a large stump, smoothly\r\nsawed; this was their writing table, on which they spread their maps\r\nand journals. Captain Clark had traded with the Indians for a panther\r\nskin seven feet long; this made a good rug. York occupied the same\r\ncabin. Chaboneau was the captains\xe2\x80\x99 cook; he and Sa-ca-ja-we-a and\r\nlittle Toussaint lived in another room, built on. The men were divided\r\ninto four messes, each with a cook, and the supplies were doled out\r\nfrom the storehouse every morning.\r\n\r\nDrouillard, the chief hunter, and George Shannon, John Collins,\r\nFrancois Labiche and Reuben Fields were sent out to hunt for elk and\r\ndeer; but the meat spoiled so quickly, even although smoked, in this\r\ndamp climate, that Joe Fields, William Bratton, Alec Willard, George\r\nGibson and Peter Wiser were ordered to the seashore with kettles, to\r\nmake salt.\r\n\r\nThey built a furnace or fireplace, of stones, and boiled down\r\nkettlesful of salt water. They brought back a gallon of good salt, for\r\ntable use and for preserving the meat. All winter the salt-makers were\r\nkept at work. Peter served his turn.\r\n\r\nThe hunters were constantly out, chasing elk over the bogs. The meat\r\nnot eaten was salted and smoke-dried; from the tallow, candles were\r\nrun, in reed moulds; and from the hides the men made shirts and\r\ntrousers and moccasins, in preparation for the next journey. The\r\ncaptains determined that the whole party should return by land, as soon\r\nas the travel season opened. No ship was to be expected.\r\n\r\nThe captains led out exploring parties. Captain Clark gained a great\r\nreputation as a shot; with a single ball no larger than a pea he\r\nclipped off the heads of geese and ducks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKloshe musquet! Kum-tux musquet!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Indians. \xe2\x80\x9cVery good\r\nmusket! Do not understand this kind of musket!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTheir own guns were rusty flint-locks, loaded with poor powder and\r\ngravel. Their bows were beautiful and true, but were not strong enough\r\nfor killing elk. They were not nearly so strong as the bows of the\r\nOtoes and the Sioux, decided Peter; not nearly so strong as his own\r\nMandan bow.\r\n\r\nThe Indians from all around visited the fort. The Chinooks, under Chief\r\nCom-com-ly, who had only one eye (\xe2\x80\x9cSame as me,\xe2\x80\x9d chuckled Cruzatte),\r\nlived on the north side of the bay; on this south side lived the\r\nClatsops, under Chief Co-bo-way. Nearer the sea lived the Tilla-mooks.\r\nUp the Columbia River lived Cath-lam-ets. These all looked much alike,\r\nbeing small, ugly, and flat-footed and crooked-legged from squatting so\r\nmuch in their canoes and by their fires.\r\n\r\nThey were well acquainted with white men. One squaw had the name \xe2\x80\x9cJ.\r\nBowman\xe2\x80\x9d tattooed on her arm. The captain spent much time talking with\r\nthem, and learned of the ships and the white traders who had been in\r\nhere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTyee (chief) Haley; so many mast (and Chief Com-com-ly held up three\r\nfingers); stay long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCallalamet; wood leg; trader.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTyee Davidson; three mast; hunt elk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so forth, all of which the captains, particularly Captain Lewis,\r\ncarefully wrote down.\r\n\r\nThe visitors brought provisions and goods to trade: fish, a little elk\r\nand deer, high-crowned hats woven of grass and bark, grass bowls that\r\nheld water, so tight they were; grass mats, furs. Some of the chiefs\r\nwore splendid robes of sea-otter skin. These were priced very dear, for\r\nthe Indians were shrewd traders. They wanted fish-hooks, knives, and\r\nfiles, in exchange for ordinary articles; but only blue beads would buy\r\nthe otter-skin robes.\r\n\r\nFor one otter-skin robe Captain Clark offered a watch, a handkerchief,\r\na dollar, and a bunch of red beads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! Tyee ka-mo-suck!\xe2\x80\x9d refused the Indian. \xe2\x80\x9cChief beads.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Sa-ca-ja-we-a gave to the captain her own girdle of blue \xe2\x80\x9cchief\r\nbeads,\xe2\x80\x9d and for it he bought a robe.\r\n\r\nThere were several new roots that the men grew to like. One root,\r\nsha-na-taw-hee, was a thistle root, purple after it had been roasted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTastes like a parsnip, only swater,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Pat.\r\n\r\nAnother root was cul-whay-ma; two feet long and slender. It also was\r\nsweet and wholesome. But the best root was the wappatoo\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x9ca rale Irish\r\np\xe2\x80\x99tatie,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pat.\r\n\r\nThis was brought down by Skilloots and the Wah-ki-a-cums, from\r\nup-river. It was a species of lily, and grew in the lakes. The Indian\r\nwomen waded in, breast-deep, and poking with their toes loosened the\r\nbulbs, which rose then to the surface. That was cold work.\r\n\r\nThe wappatoo roots were held at a rather stiff figure, because they\r\ncould be traded to the other Indians, if not to the white men.\r\n\r\nThe Clatsops were the best Indians. The Cath-lam-ets were treacherous;\r\none would have killed Hugh McNeal had not a Chinook woman warned Hugh.\r\nThe Chinooks were thievish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo Chinook shall be admitted into the fort without special invitation,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nfinally ordered Captain Lewis.\r\n\r\nSo after that when Indians appeared outside they always shouted: \xe2\x80\x9cNo\r\nChinook. Clatsop.\xe2\x80\x9d Or \xe2\x80\x9cSkilloot,\xe2\x80\x9d or whatever they chanced to be or\r\npretended to be. Another order was issued that no Indians should remain\r\nin the fort over night.\r\n\r\nThe Indians brought many fleas, too\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x9cthe wan thing for which we\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nnothin\xe2\x80\x99 to trade,\xe2\x80\x9d as said Pat.\r\n\r\nThe greatest excitement of the winter was the arrival of a whale. Chief\r\nCo-bo-way of the Clatsops came with the news, and also with three dogs\r\nand some blubber. He said that the whale had been stranded ashore near\r\nthe Tillamooks\xe2\x80\x99 village down the coast. He was given a pair of old\r\nsatin breeches, and went away much pleased.\r\n\r\nJoe Fields and George Gibson appeared at the fort with the gallon of\r\nsalt from the salt camp, and with some more of the whale blubber. They\r\nsaid that the Indians all were flocking to the whale and cutting it up.\r\nThe blubber, when cooked, looked and tasted like beaver tail\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95it was\r\nvery good; and Captain Clark immediately organized a party to go to\r\nthe spot and get what blubber they might.\r\n\r\nNaturally, everybody was anxious to see the whale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better take Peter, hadn\xe2\x80\x99t you, Captain?\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Captain\r\nLewis. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a boy\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95he ought to see what there is to be seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Captain Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what a whale is,\r\nPeter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA big fish,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Peter, eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; a big warm-blooded fish; a fish bigger than a buffalo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow, Sa-ca-ja-we-a had heard; she had helped Chaboneau cook the blubber\r\nfor the captains. But she had not been invited to go. In fact, all this\r\ntime the Bird-woman had not been even so far as the big water. She had\r\nworked in the fort.\r\n\r\nSuddenly she did a very surprising thing, for an Indian woman. When she\r\nbelieved that she was to be left out of the sightseeing party, she wept.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy you want to go?\xe2\x80\x9d scolded Chaboneau. \xe2\x80\x9cZe capitaines no haf time to\r\nwait for woman with baby. You stay by ze lodge fire; dat is place for\r\nwomans.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSa-ca-ja-we-a tilted her chin at him and went straight to Captain Clark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCapitin! I speak a leetle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, Sa-ca-ja-we-a?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI come long way, capitin. I carry baby, I cold, hungry, wet, seeck,\r\nI keep up an\xe2\x80\x99 I no complain. I show you trail; when you no know which\r\nway, I say \xe2\x80\x98Snake people here,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 you find Snakes. When Indians see\r\nme, dey say: \xe2\x80\x98Dis no war party,\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 dey kind to you. When you get\r\nhungry for bread, I gif you one leetle bit I carry all way from Mandan\r\ntown, so you can taste. When you want otter robe, I gif you my belt,\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 you get otter robe. I been here all dis time, an\xe2\x80\x99 I not yet go near\r\nde big water dat I travel many days to see. Now dere is a big fish;\r\nodders go, Chaboneau say I mus\xe2\x80\x99 stay an\xe2\x80\x99 care for Toussaint an\xe2\x80\x99 help\r\ncook. I feel bad, capitin\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95I\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95I\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x9d and poor little Bird-woman hid her\r\nface in her shawl and sobbed.\r\n\r\nThe captain placed his hand kindly upon her shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall go, Sa-ca-ja-we-a. You shall go with us and see the ocean\r\nand the big fish; and Chaboneau can stay by the fire and tend to the\r\nbaby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSa-ca-ja-we-a smiled and dried her eyes. Very proud, she made ready.\r\nBut Chaboneau went, too\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95because he, likewise, wished to inspect the\r\ngreat wonder which had been cast ashore.\r\n\r\nThe whale was 105 feet long. The busy Indians had stripped it to the\r\nbones, and with difficulty Captain Clark managed to buy 300 pounds of\r\nblubber and some oil.\r\n\r\nThus, with hunting, trading, and making garments of leather, the winter\r\npassed. An astonishingly mild winter it was, too, of little frost and\r\nwet snow, but of much rain and fog which gave the men rheumatism, and\r\nwhich, by spoiling the food and cutting down exercise, gave them boils\r\nand stomach complaint, also.\r\n\r\nThe captains were constantly hoping for a ship and fresh supplies. None\r\nwas sighted.\r\n\r\nSo February merged with March. The elk were retiring from the low\r\ncountry to the high, following the grass. On some days the fort had\r\nonly one day\xe2\x80\x99s provisions in store.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can find no elk, notting,\xe2\x80\x9d complained Drouillard, the chief hunter.\r\n\r\nThe Indians hoarded their own food very close, to make it last until\r\nthe salmon began to run again, in the spring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSix blue blankets, wan red wan, five striped wans that used to be our\r\nbig United States flag, some old breeches an\xe2\x80\x99 waistcuts, an\xe2\x80\x99 Cap\xe2\x80\x99n\r\nClark\xe2\x80\x99s artillery dress-coat an\xe2\x80\x99 hat\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95faith, that\xe2\x80\x99s all we\xe2\x80\x99ve got an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nat prisent prices they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t buy a square meal,\xe2\x80\x9d reported Patrick\r\nGass. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll be atin\xe2\x80\x99 ourselves naked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDose t\xe2\x80\x99ings be need\xe2\x80\x99 for boats an\xe2\x80\x99 hosses,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cruzatte. \xe2\x80\x9cOf de\r\nleetle t\xe2\x80\x99ings we haf scarce one hat full. How we go back four t\xe2\x80\x99ousand\r\nmiles I do not know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVII\r\n\r\nFRIENDLY YELLEPT, THE WALLA WALLA\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrouillard,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke Captain Lewis, \xe2\x80\x9cwe must have another canoe. These\r\nIndians down here won\xe2\x80\x99t sell us any. Try what you can do up the river.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was the middle of March. The captains had intended to wait until\r\nat least the first of April, before starting on the back trail, so as\r\nnot to arrive at the mountains until June. Then the snows would have\r\nmelted, and there would be game. But meat already was extremely scarce\r\naround Fort Clatsop; the expedition would better start at once, and\r\nhunt along the way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI try de Cath-lam-et\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95dey haf canoes,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Drouillard. \xe2\x80\x9cBut dey\r\nwill hol\xe2\x80\x99 dem dear. I t\xe2\x80\x99ink I must take de best t\xe2\x80\x99ings we haf. Mebbe\r\nyou let me take your lace coat, capitaine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! My only dress uniform?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy not that\r\nartillery coat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that\xe2\x80\x99s mine!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Captain Clark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne day a Cath-lam-et see your lace coat an\xe2\x80\x99 like it. I sure I get\r\ncanoe for it,\xe2\x80\x9d persisted Drouillard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cAnother canoe we must have. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nhold councils in my leather clothes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the canny Drouillard, who was half Indian himself, went up the\r\nCath-lam-ets and traded the laced dress-coat for a canoe.\r\n\r\nSergeant Pat was ordered to count the moccasins in stock. He reported\r\n338 pairs, manufactured during the winter from the hides of the 131 elk\r\nand twenty deer that had been killed.\r\n\r\nTo Chief Co-bo-way (or Com-mo-wool), of the Clatsops, was given the\r\nfort and all its furniture. He had been exceedingly friendly; and now\r\nhe appeared to appreciate the gift very much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will make my home in the house where the white chiefs lived,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ndeclared.\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis and Captain Clark and several of the men had long before\r\ncarved their names into trees, as a record for other white men to see.\r\nAnd there, on a rock, also was \xe2\x80\x9cPETER.\xe2\x80\x9d During the winter Peter had\r\nmade great progress in reading and writing. However, something more\r\nofficial and explanatory than only inscriptions on trees was needed,\r\nthat the trading ships which came in might know and might carry\r\nthe news to the world. Therefore the captains wrote out statements\r\ncontaining the names of the party and maps of the country explored. The\r\nnotices said:\r\n\r\n    The object of this list is, that through the medium of some\r\n    civilized person, who may see the same, it may be made known to\r\n    the world that the party consisting of the persons whose names\r\n    are hereunto affixed, and who were sent out by the government\r\n    of the United States to explore the interior of the continent\r\n    of North America, did penetrate the same by the way of the\r\n    Missouri and Columbia Rivers, to the discharge of the latter\r\n    into the Pacific Ocean, where they arrived on the 14th day of\r\n    November, 1805, and departed the 23rd day of March, 1806, on\r\n    their return to the United States, by the same route by which\r\n    they had come out.\r\n\r\nOne copy was pasted up on a smooth post in the headquarters cabin.\r\nOther copies were given to the Clatsops and the Chinooks, who promised\r\nto hand them to white traders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, we\xe2\x80\x99ll beat the news home,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted Sergeant Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cFor the\r\nships\xe2\x80\x99ll be a long time makin\xe2\x80\x99 it, by Chiny an\xe2\x80\x99 the inds o\xe2\x80\x99 the world,\r\nwhile it\xe2\x80\x99s straight across we go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd this proved truth. Had the captains only known, at the very time\r\nthe notices were being written, the American trading brig Lydia, of\r\nBoston, Captain Hill, was cruising along the coast, and in the first\r\nweek of April anchored in the mouth of the Columbia. But the other\r\nAmericans had been gone two weeks, and Chief Coboway was ruler of Fort\r\nClatsop. So Captain Hill took one of the statements, carried it to\r\nChina with him, and delivered it at Boston not until May, 1807.\r\n\r\nAt 1 o\xe2\x80\x99clock of March 23, this 1806, Fort Clatsop was abandoned; out\r\ninto the little river that flowed past it the five canoes glided, and\r\nheaded down for the Columbia\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95thence eastward which was _homeward_!\r\n\r\nThe men swung their hats, of tattered felt, of furs, and of Chinook\r\nweave from grass and bark; and cheered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDe nex\xe2\x80\x99 winter we spen\xe2\x80\x99 in de United States,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoiced Cruzatte. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nplay my feedle at Cahokia an\xe2\x80\x99 make de pleasure dere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve come away with plenty powder and lead, and plenty salt; that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\none good job,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Pat.\r\n\r\nThe powder, sealed in lead canisters, had kept splendidly. Now there\r\nwere 140 pounds of it. And as to salt\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95twelve gallons had been packed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s been not such a bad winter, after all, even if we did have only\r\nsix clear days in six months,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed George Shannon. \xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99ll soon\r\nbe rid of our rheumatism.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSpring had arrived; for although the weather continued wet and raw,\r\nwild fowl were feeding in the ponds, the gooseberry and honeysuckle\r\nwere leaving forth in the parks, and the frogs were croaking in the\r\nmarshes. Many Indians were met; they were gathering along the river, to\r\nwait for the salmon to run up from the sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext full moon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Indians. \xe2\x80\x9cNo salmon till next full moon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe second of May, that is,\xe2\x80\x9d figured Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, we can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwait. We\xe2\x80\x99ll have to depend on our guns; for if we wait, winter will\r\novertake us on the Missouri. Where there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to shoot, we can\r\nlive for a time on dogs and horses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Indians seemed poor and starving. Captain Clark was told of a\r\nlarge river emptying from the south: the Multnomah, which is the\r\nWillamette. He ascended it a short distance, and there found some of\r\nthe Neer-cho-ki-oo tribe. They refused to sell him any wappatoo roots.\r\nBut he tossed a match into a fire; it blazed and frightened them. He\r\nplaced a magnet on his compass, and whirled the compass needle \xe2\x80\x99round\r\nand \xe2\x80\x99round. The women and children crawled under the bed-covers, and\r\nthe men piled wappatoo roots at his feet. The captain liked to do this\r\nsort of thing.\r\n\r\nHe returned from among the Multnomahs with roots and five dogs.\r\n\r\nThe Indians were not all friendly, especially those new tribes who had\r\ntraveled to await the salmon. The Clah-clel-lahs threw stones at the\r\ncanoes, and stole things; John Shields had to defend himself with his\r\nhunting-knife. The Wah-clel-lahs stole the little black Assiniboine\r\ndog. Captain Lewis, who was very fond of the little dog, immediately\r\nsent Sergeant Pryor, Drouillard and Hugh McNeal to get it even if they\r\nhad to shoot the thieves. The thieves ran off and left the dog. And in\r\nthe village of the Skilloots Captain Lewis knocked down an Indian who\r\nwas carrying off a valuable piece of iron.\r\n\r\nAmong the Skilloots, here, quite a number of articles were lost; so\r\nthat Captain Lewis made a speech, to say that he and his men were not\r\nafraid and were able to burn the village if necessary to stop the\r\nthieving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYessuh! Dese hyah Galloots\xe2\x80\x99d better watch out,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed York. \xe2\x80\x9cMarse\r\nMerne an\xe2\x80\x99 Marse Will are offishurs of the \xe2\x80\x99Nited States ahmy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHowever, from the Skilloots ten horses were purchased with blankets\r\nand Captain Clark\xe2\x80\x99s artillery coat and two kettles, and two more were\r\nborrowed. William Bratton was too ill to walk, and rode one of the\r\nhorses. Nine others were loaded with the baggage, to take it around the\r\nrapids. One horse was stolen, and Captain Clark rode the twelfth up to\r\nthe village of the E-nee-shurs.\r\n\r\nThree of the canoes were broken up for fuel. The captains hoped soon to\r\ntravel altogether by horses; canoe work, against the current, was slow,\r\nhard work.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn amazin\xe2\x80\x99 disagrayable people,\xe2\x80\x9d commented Sergeant Pat, on the\r\nSkilloots. \xe2\x80\x9cBut Twisted-hair and his Pierced Noses\xe2\x80\x99ll be gintlemen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe E-nee-shurs were no better in manners and honesty. The horse\r\nChaboneau was leading ran away, and spilled his pack; an E-nee-shur\r\nmade off with a fine robe, and before it was returned Captain Lewis had\r\nto utter more threats.\r\n\r\nAll in all, the trip up-river was very vexing, until, finally having\r\ncollected enough horses for the baggage, so as to do without any\r\ncanoes, the party arrived on April 27 at the Walla Walla village where\r\nlived Chief Yellept who last October had wanted them to stay longer\r\nwith him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will visit you on our way back,\xe2\x80\x9d had promised Captain Clark. Now\r\nhere they were\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and Chief Yellept was glad indeed to see them.\r\n\r\nHe met them a few miles below the village.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome and stay with me three or four days,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to the captains.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall have more horses, and plenty food. I am wearing the little\r\nmedal given me from my white father; I hope that you will give me a\r\nbigger one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe village was six miles above, opposite the mouth of the Walla Walla\r\nRiver. Chief Yellept made good his word. He called his people together,\r\nto tell them that they must be hospitable to the white strangers; and\r\nhe set an example by bringing the captains an armful of wood and a\r\nplatter of three baked fish. Then all the Walla Walla squaws busied\r\nthemselves with gathering wood for their guests. Dogs were offered at\r\nreasonable prices.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDese Wallow-wallows \xe2\x80\x99mos\xe2\x80\x99 like home folks,\xe2\x80\x9d declared York.\r\n\r\nForsooth, it was difficult to get away from the village, so friendly\r\nwere Chief Yellept\xe2\x80\x99s people. The chief appeared to have taken a great\r\nfancy to the Red Head, and presented him with a noble white horse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the Red Head will give me a kettle, for my lodge, I will be happy,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Yellept.\r\n\r\nAmong the Walla Wallas there was a Snake Indian prisoner, with whom\r\nSa-ca-ja-we-a, much to her delight, could talk in Sho-sho-ne; and the\r\nSnake could translate for her the Walla Walla speech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell the Sho-sho-ne to tell Chief Yellept that we have no kettles to\r\ngive,\xe2\x80\x9d directed Captain Clark, to the little Bird-woman. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we will\r\nbe pleased to give him something else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYellept say he take what you gif,\xe2\x80\x9d interpreted Sa-ca-ja-we-a.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a fine fellow. You\xe2\x80\x99ll have to give him your sword, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsuggested Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s been wanting it, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Believe I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t transfer it to better\r\nhands,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Captain Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the last of my official garb,\r\nMerne\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and you haven\xe2\x80\x99t much left yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChief Yellept\xe2\x80\x99s eyes shone as he accepted the prized \xe2\x80\x9clong knife\xe2\x80\x9d; and\r\nshone again when to it were added powder and a hundred bullets for his\r\ngun. Now he was a big chief, indeed.\r\n\r\nThe Bird-woman had spread the word that the white chiefs were great\r\nworkers in medicine: with their magic box and their wonderful knowledge\r\nthey healed all sicknesses. Now to Captain Clark and Captain Lewis\r\nthe Walla Wallas brought broken arms, stiff knees, and sore eyes, for\r\ntreatment. The captains did their best.\r\n\r\nNot until the second morning, following a grand dance by the Indians,\r\nat the camp, might the expedition start onward. Chief Yellept had\r\ninformed them of a short cut, across country, from the mouth of the\r\nWalla Walla River to the Pierced Nose country at the Kooskooskee; a\r\nSkilloot, who had been guiding the expedition by land, said that he\r\nknew the trail, and a Pierced Nose who, with his family, was returning\r\nhome from a visit below, volunteered to help also; Chief Yellept lent\r\nthe captains two canoes, for crossing the Columbia to the south side at\r\nthe mouth of the Walla Walla, where the new trail began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe most hospitable, honest and sincere Indians we have met since\r\nleaving the United States, Merne,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted Captain Clark, when they\r\nhad been overtaken, a day\xe2\x80\x99s journey out, by three Walla Walla young men\r\nwho had hastened after to restore to them a beaver-trap that had been\r\nforgotten.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXVIII\r\n\r\nTHE PIERCED NOSES AGAIN\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe white men are coming back! The white men are coming!\xe2\x80\x9d sped the\r\nglad word among the Cho-pun-nish or Pierced Noses, in their villages\r\n100 miles up, on the Kooskooskee. \xe2\x80\x9cThey will make us well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd the white men were indeed coming, by the trail from the Walla Walla,\r\nwith the Snake Indian prisoner and Sa-ca-ja-we-a as interpreters; with\r\nthe Skilloot and the three Walla Walla young men as guides (for the\r\nPierced Nose and family had taken another trail); with some twenty\r\nhorses, for the baggage, and for William Bratton, and for the men who\r\nhad sore feet; and with the healing medicine box containing, especially,\r\nthe celebrated eye-water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us wance get the horses we left with Twisted-hair an\xe2\x80\x99 we\xe2\x80\x99ll all\r\nride, b\xe2\x80\x99gorry,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Sergeant Pat, limping along.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn ze Kamass Prairie dere will be plenty root, plenty game,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoiced\r\nChaboneau. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 mebbe dere we rest, while leetle Toussaint get well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nFor little Toussaint seemed to be ailing.\r\n\r\nFirst they were met, before reaching any village, by an old friend,\r\nChief We-ah-koo-nut, and ten warriors. We-ah-koo-nut was called the\r\nBighorn, because he always wore, hanging from his left arm, the horn\r\nof a mountain ram.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have heard that you were coming, and have ridden to greet you,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Bighorn. \xe2\x80\x9cThe sight of you makes our sore eyes well. We have\r\nno food for you here, but to-morrow you will reach a lodge where\r\neverything will be supplied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBefore breakfast, in the morning, the lodge was found, on the bank of\r\nthe Lewis or Snake River; but the families living there could supply\r\nonly two dogs and some root bread.\r\n\r\nNext was met Chief Tetoh, or Sky\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95the honest fellow who, with\r\nTwisted-hair, had helped the expedition get through from the Kamass\r\nPrairie to the Timm falls of the Columbia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlad to see you. You are welcome,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Tetoh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is Chief Twisted-hair? We have come to visit our friends, the\r\nPierced Noses, again, and to get our horses,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Captain Lewis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must cross the Kin-oo-e-nim (Snake River), here, and go to\r\nthe Kooskooskee,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Chief Tetoh. \xe2\x80\x9cThere you will find the\r\nTwisted-hair, who has your horses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo they crossed, in canoes lent to them by Tetoh, and arrived at the\r\nKooskooskee or Clearwater.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEye-water, eye-water,\xe2\x80\x9d begged the Indians. Captain Clark traded a\r\nsmall bottle of the eye-water for a gray mare.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re the doctor, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cFrom now on we\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbetter charge a fee. We\xe2\x80\x99ll get more meat that way than with our guns or\r\ngoods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAccordingly Captain Clark, who handled the medicines, exchanged his\r\nservices for provisions. But the Indians appeared to be very poor, and\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cdoctor\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x9d fees in dogs and horses and roots did not amount to much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarse Will won\xe2\x80\x99t nebber make a libbin\xe2\x80\x99 at doctorin\xe2\x80\x99, dat\xe2\x80\x99s suah,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nfinally admitted York, with a shake of his head. \xe2\x80\x9cAnyhow, he ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nkilled anybody yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChief Twisted-hair\xe2\x80\x99s village was up the Kooskooskee some miles. Chief\r\nSky, and another chief named Cut-nose, rode along with the captains.\r\nWhen questioned about the horses and the saddles, they would give no\r\nstraight answer; but\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cS\xe2\x80\x99pose no get \xe2\x80\x99um horse, no get \xe2\x80\x99um saddle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sa-ca-ja-we-a.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSho-sho-ne say he hear saddles gone, horses gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was alarming news.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 Twisted-hair seemed like a fine gintleman,\xe2\x80\x9d bemoaned Sergeant Pat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can get more horses, can\xe2\x80\x99t we, Pat?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Peter. \xe2\x80\x9cWe see lots of\r\nhorses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, an\xe2\x80\x99 how\xe2\x80\x99ll we buy \xe2\x80\x99em, when each man of us is down to a couple o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nneedles, a bit of thread an\xe2\x80\x99 a yard or so of ribbon, with a pinch o\xe2\x80\x99\r\npaint for an extry?\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have to cut the buttons off\r\nour clothes, I guess. Cross the mountains on foot ag\xe2\x80\x99in we won\xe2\x80\x99t an\xe2\x80\x99\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t. They\xe2\x80\x99re waist-deep in snow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the mountains were looming ahead, white and wintry, although this\r\nwas May.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Twisted-hair,\xe2\x80\x9d announced Chief Sky, pointing before. And Chief\r\nTwisted-hair, with six men, met the procession.\r\n\r\nTwisted-hair was not at all in a good humor. He refused to shake hands,\r\nhe scarcely noticed the captains, and suddenly he and Cut-nose (a very\r\nugly man whose nose had been laid open by a Snake lance, in battle)\r\nwere quarreling in a loud voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s this all about, Chaboneau?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cAsk\r\nSa-ca-ja-we-a to have the Sho-sho-ne interpret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cZe Sho-sho-ne will not,\xe2\x80\x9d reported Chaboneau. \xe2\x80\x9cHe say dees is quarrel\r\nbetween two chiefs an\xe2\x80\x99 he haf no right to interfere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll go on a bit and camp and hold a council, Will,\xe2\x80\x9d directed Captain\r\nLewis to Captain Clark. \xe2\x80\x9cThen we\xe2\x80\x99ll get at the bottom of this business.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s evidently something wrong with the horses and saddles we left.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt camp the captains first smoked and talked with Twisted-hair. He said\r\nit was true that the horses were scattered, but Cut-nose and another\r\nchief, the Broken-arm, were to blame. They had been jealous of him\r\nbecause he had the white men\xe2\x80\x99s horses; and being an old man, he had\r\ngiven up the horses. Some were near, and some were at the village of\r\nthe Broken-arm, a half-day\xe2\x80\x99s march east. As for the saddles, the cache\r\nhad fallen in and they might have been stolen, but he had hidden them\r\nagain.\r\n\r\nThen the Cut-nose talked. He said that the Twisted-hair was a bad old\r\nman, of two faces; that he had not taken care of the horses but had\r\nlet his young men ride them, to hunt, until the Broken-arm, who was a\r\nhigher chief, and he, Cut-nose, had forbidden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not well that the chiefs quarrel,\xe2\x80\x9d reproved Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly\r\nchildren quarrel. We will take what horses there are here and we will\r\ngo on to the village of the Broken-arm, for the other horses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis seemed to satisfy everybody. Twisted-hair\xe2\x80\x99s young men brought in\r\ntwenty-one of the forty-three horses and half the saddles, besides some\r\nof the powder and lead that had been buried, also. That night Cut-nose\r\nand Twisted-hair slept together.\r\n\r\nThe Broken-arm and his Nez Perc\xc3\xa9s lived in one large straw-and-mud\r\nhouse 150 feet long. Over it was flying the United States flag that had\r\nbeen given to the nation on the way down last fall. Broken-arm ordered\r\na hide tent erected for the white chiefs; his women hastened there with\r\nroots and fish; and when the captains offered to trade a lean horse for\r\na fat one which might be killed, Broken-arm declined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen our guests come hungry, we do not sell them food,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have many young horses. All those you see on these plains belong\r\nto me and my people. Take what you need for food.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNiver before did we have the Injuns offer us somethin\xe2\x80\x99 for nothin\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ngasped Patrick Gass. \xe2\x80\x9cAt laste, niver before were we told to go help\r\nourselves!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Walla Wallas were as obliging. Don\xe2\x80\x99t forget the Walla Wallas, and\r\nYellept,\xe2\x80\x9d reminded George Shannon.\r\n\r\nTwo weeks were spent near the big house of the Broken-arm, for whom\r\nanother name was Black Eagle. Captain Clark was appointed official\r\ndoctor; he had fifty patients at a time. Captain Lewis held a council,\r\nand told the warriors about the United States. They promised to make\r\npeace with the Sho-sho-nes. Labiche killed a bear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese are great hunters. They kill the bear, alone,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the\r\nPierced Noses.\r\n\r\nHunters were sent out every day, to get bear, and deer, and\r\nelk\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95whatever they could. The other men were sent out to trade for\r\nroots and fish.\r\n\r\nLittle Toussaint grew better. William Bratton could not walk, but he\r\nwas put into a hut of boughs and blankets built over a hole in which\r\nthere had been a fire. Water was sprinkled into the hole. The hot\r\nsteam soaked William through and through. He was then plunged into\r\ncold water, and sweated again in the hut. This was Indian treatment,\r\nnot white man\xe2\x80\x99s. And it cured Bratton, after even Doctor Red Head had\r\nfailed.\r\n\r\nMost of the saddles and all the horses except two were delivered. These\r\ntwo, said Broken-arm, had been stolen last fall by old Toby and his son\r\non their way back to Chief Ca-me-ah-wait. There now were sixty-five\r\nhorses on hand\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95enough for the baggage and for the men. Everybody\r\nmight ride. So much food had been purchased, that buttons (as Pat had\r\npredicted) were being traded in, and John Shields, blacksmith, was\r\nmaking awls out of the links of a beaver-trap chain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must start on, or we won\xe2\x80\x99t reach Fort Mandan before winter,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nannounced Captain Lewis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d objected Twisted-hair and Sky, and all. \xe2\x80\x9cToo much snow. Much\r\nwater come down. The trail over the mountains is not open. Wait till\r\nthe next full moon, and the snows will have melted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe salmon will soon be running up the river. Wait, and you shall have\r\nfood,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cut-nose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the white chiefs are hungry, let them kill and eat my horses,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nChief Ho-has-til-pilp, the Red Wolf, with a wave of his arm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe thank the Red Wolf. But we shall need guides. Will the chiefs send\r\nsome young men with us, to show us the way over the mountains?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nCaptain Lewis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen there is grass for the horses, on the Road-to-the-Buffalo, we\r\nwill send young men,\xe2\x80\x9d promised Chief Broken-arm. \xe2\x80\x9cBut not until after\r\nthe grand council of all the Pierced Nose nation, on the Kamass\r\nPrairie. In the summer we will all go to the buffalo plains of the\r\nMissouri, if the white chiefs will protect us from the Snakes and\r\nPahkees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold high the peace flag we have given you, and it will turn your\r\nenemies into friends,\xe2\x80\x9d instructed Captain Lewis.\r\n\r\nThe Grand Council was not to be held for two or three weeks yet. By the\r\nclose of the first week of June the river had fallen six feet, showing\r\nthat the snows were partially melted. The captains decided to push\r\nalong without guides.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe cannot wait till July and the full moon, boys,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Captain\r\nLewis, in an address to the company. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only 160 miles from the\r\nKamass Prairie to our old camp on the other side at Traveler\xe2\x80\x99s Rest\r\nCreek, and there we\xe2\x80\x99ll be done with the snow. If no guides overtake us,\r\nDrouillard and Labiche and some of the rest of you are as good trailers\r\nas the Indians, and can lead us through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHooray!\xe2\x80\x9d cheered all. They were as anxious as the captains to go.\r\nThey were in fine fettle. They had been playing prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s base, among\r\nthemselves, and had been running foot-races with the Nez Perc\xc3\xa9s, to\r\nharden their muscles. In the races only one Indian had proved as fast\r\nas Peter and John Colter, the American champions.\r\n\r\nNow on June 10 camp was broken, and the march to the mountains begun.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen days\xe2\x80\x99ll see us through,\xe2\x80\x9d confidently declared Pat.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIX\r\n\r\nBACK ACROSS THE MOUNTAINS\r\n\r\n\r\nTraveler\xe2\x80\x99s Rest Creek, at last! But Pat\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cten days\xe2\x80\x9d had lengthened\r\ninto twenty, for this was June 29.\r\n\r\nThere had been good reason. To be sure, the Kamass Prairie had been\r\nfound all abloom with the kamass, so that the host of pale petals had\r\nmade it look like a lake. The wild roses were in flower; the ground\r\nsquirrels were busy, and supplied tender tidbits. But when the company\r\ntried to climb they encountered snow fifteen feet deep, covering the\r\ngrass and the trail, and the air was that of winter. Game was very\r\nscarce.\r\n\r\nThe captains shook their heads, and called a council of the company.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t go on in this fashion, men,\xe2\x80\x9d said Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cAlready\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re short of food, and so are the horses. Even if we knew the trail,\r\nand could travel at our best, we\xe2\x80\x99ve four days yet until we reach grass\r\non the other side. If we lost the trail, in the snow, we\xe2\x80\x99d be lost,\r\ntoo. So Captain Clark and I have decided that we all must return to the\r\nKamass Prairie, kill more meat, and see if the Nez Perc\xc3\xa9s won\xe2\x80\x99t furnish\r\nus with guides. The snow holds the horses up, and with experienced\r\nguides we can make good time. Failing of guides, we\xe2\x80\x99ll try again,\r\nanyway\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95sending our best woodsmen ahead to note the marks on the\r\ntrees and to blaze the trail. But first, Drouillard and Shannon will\r\nstart back immediately, to the Nez Perc\xc3\xa9 grand council, which is now\r\nin session, and offer two guns for some guides. They\xe2\x80\x99ll join us on the\r\nprairie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis sounded sensible, although everybody did hate to retrace steps.\r\nThe going down, amidst snow-hidden rocks and timber, was cruel work.\r\n\r\nDrouillard and George Shannon were gone for almost a week. When they\r\nreappeared they brought three young Nez Perc\xc3\xa9s warriors as guides. Then\r\na quick trip was made. The first day out the guides set fire to the\r\ntimber, in order, they said, to \xe2\x80\x9cmake fair weather.\xe2\x80\x9d They led rapidly.\r\nThey never missed the trail. Whenever the snow thinned, in spots,\r\nthere, underfoot, was the trail, plain to be seen\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95the great Nez Perc\xc3\xa9\r\nRoad-to-the-Buffalo, from the west of the mountains to the east. Even\r\nDrouillard and Sa-ca-ja-we-a exclaimed with approval of such accurate\r\nguiding.\r\n\r\nAll the old camps of the fall before were passed. The Hungry Creek\r\ncamp, where Captain Clark had left the horse hung up, and where Peter\r\nand Reuben Fields had supped on the horse\xe2\x80\x99s head; the camp of September\r\n17, from which Captain Clark had set out ahead to find the Nez Perc\xc3\xa9s;\r\nthe camp of September 16, where the spotted colt was killed; the camp\r\nof September 14, where the black colt was killed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, I\xe2\x80\x99m glad we\xe2\x80\x99re goin\xe2\x80\x99 the other way,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve no\r\npleasant recollections of the first trip, when we were afoot an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nstarvin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd the other men agreed with him.\r\n\r\nOn the fifth day the mountains had been crossed. On the sixth day the\r\nsnow had ceased, and the head of Traveler\xe2\x80\x99s Rest Creek was reached.\r\nOn the next day, June 30, they hastened down the creek, and soon were\r\ncamped again at its mouth\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95the camping spot of September 11, before!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere we are, back in the Missouri country, boys,\xe2\x80\x9d cheered Captain\r\nClark. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve been clear through to the Pacific and not lost a man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 nebber killed an Injun,\xe2\x80\x9d added York. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we mighty nigh had to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay have a fight yet,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth George Gibson. \xe2\x80\x9cWe ought to have met some\r\nof the Oo-tla-shoots hereabouts. The guides are afraid to go on. They\r\nclaim their friends have been wiped out by the Pahkees or Blackfeet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDey much \xe2\x80\x99fraid,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke Drouillard. \xe2\x80\x9cDey see de tracks of two Injuns\r\nbarefoot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Peter himself knew, Indians who were barefoot were likely to be\r\nIndians in distress.\r\n\r\nHowever, the captains did not appear to be alarmed. The news was spread\r\nthat the company were to be divided. Captain Clark and party were to\r\ntravel southward, along this, the east side of the mountains, get the\r\ncanoes and other stuff where they had been hidden at the first meeting\r\nplace with Chief Ca-me-ah-wait\xe2\x80\x99s Sho-sho-nes. Then half the party,\r\nunder Sergeant Ordway, were to descend the Jefferson, from there,\r\nwith the canoes and other stuff, into the main Missouri and on to the\r\nWhite-bear Islands camp at the Great Falls.\r\n\r\nThe other half of the party, under Captain Clark, were to cross\r\neastward, by land, to the Yellowstone River, and descend that to its\r\nmouth in the Missouri.\r\n\r\nThe Captain Lewis party were to continue eastward from this present\r\ncamp on Traveler\xe2\x80\x99s Rest Creek, and try to follow the Pierced Nose\r\nRoad-to-the-Buffalo to the Great Falls of the Missouri; there they were\r\nto meet Sergeant Ordway, and at the mouth of the Yellowstone they all\r\nwere to meet Captain Clark.\r\n\r\nNow, with which party did Peter wish to go? The Captain Clark trip\r\nsounded very interesting\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95down that Yellowstone River, where no white\r\nmen had been. Sa-ca-ja-we-a was to guide him, too, across country. But\r\nthe Captain Lewis trip also sounded interesting\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95all by land, through\r\nanother unknown country, to the wonderful falls again. On this trip\r\nthere would be good hunting\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and possibly the Blackfeet Indians.\r\n\r\nThe Sergeant Ordway trip sounded the least interesting, for it meant\r\nmerely floating down the same rivers that they had toiled up.\r\n\r\nHowever, Peter was a soldier and had no choice. So he waited anxiously\r\nwhile the captains made their selections. It was like choosing sides in\r\nthe game of prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s base.\r\n\r\nFor Captain Clark: Sergeant Ordway, Sergeant Nat Pryor, John Shields,\r\nGeorge Shannon, William Bratton, Dick Windsor, George Gibson, Hugh\r\nHall, Francois Labiche, John Colter, the fast runner, John Collins,\r\nTom Howard, John Potts, Baptiste Lepage, Alex Willard, Joe Whitehouse,\r\nPeter Wiser, Old Cruzatte, York, Chaboneau, and the Bird-woman.\r\n\r\nFor Captain Lewis: Sergeant Pat, Joe Fields and Reuben Fields,\r\nDrouillard, the hunter, William Werner, Rob Frazier, Hugh McNeal, John\r\nThompson and Si Goodrich.\r\n\r\nThen where was Peter? Nobody seemed to want him. But Sergeant Pat made\r\na scrape and a salute.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeg your pardon, sorr,\xe2\x80\x9d to Captain Lewis; \xe2\x80\x9cbut are we to lave Peter\r\nhere till we come ag\xe2\x80\x99in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Pon my word!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the captain. \xe2\x80\x9cNo! He\xe2\x80\x99s to come along with us,\r\nof course. He\xe2\x80\x99s in your charge, Pat, remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYis, sorr. Thank ye, sorr,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Pat.\r\n\r\nAnd Peter was glad.\r\n\r\nSo the parties separated, Captain Clark to the south, and the place\r\nwhere the canoes and goods had been left last August; Captain Lewis to\r\nthe east and the Great Falls.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood luck, boys,\xe2\x80\x9d was the final word. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll all meet at the Missouri.\r\nThen down we\xe2\x80\x99ll go, for home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Pierced Noses who had guided across the mountains went with Captain\r\nLewis a short distance still, to show him the shortest route along\r\nthe Road-to-the-Buffalo. Before they quit, in order to look for their\r\nfriends the Oo-tla-shoots or Flat-heads, the captain gave them presents\r\nof meat, and exchanged names with the leader, who was a young chief.\r\n\r\nThe young chief was henceforth to be known as the Long Knife, and\r\nCaptain Lewis was to be known as Yo-me-kol-lick, or White Bear-skin\r\nUnfolded.\r\n\r\nIt proved to be only nine days\xe2\x80\x99 travel to the White-bear Islands camp\r\nat the head of the Falls of the Missouri, and during all the way not an\r\nIndian was sighted, although fresh sign was discovered\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x9cBlackfeet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nasserted Drouillard. \xe2\x80\x9cDe Gros-ventres of de Prairie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose Big-bellies must be bad Injuns, I\xe2\x80\x99m thinkin\xe2\x80\x99, by the way\r\neverywan\xe2\x80\x99s afraid of \xe2\x80\x99em,\xe2\x80\x9d said Pat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery bad,\xe2\x80\x9d asserted Peter. For even the Otoes of the south feared the\r\nnorthern \xe2\x80\x9cGros-ventres\xe2\x80\x9d as much as they did the Sioux.\r\n\r\nThere had been plenty of buffalo, bellowing all the nights; but there\r\nhad been a tremendous amount of mosquitoes, too, which bit so that even\r\nthe little black dog howled with pain.\r\n\r\nNow, here at the old camp were the \xe2\x80\x9cwhite bears,\xe2\x80\x9d as pugnacious as\r\nbefore. One treed Hugh McNeal and kept him treed near half a day, after\r\nHugh had broken his gun over the bear\xe2\x80\x99s head.\r\n\r\nNobody had disturbed the articles that had been left here last summer.\r\nSome things had spoiled from dampness; but the frame of the iron canoe\r\nwas all right, and so were the cottonwood wagon-wheels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGass, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to leave you in charge, here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the captain. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nwill wait till the Ordway party come with the canoes; then you will\r\nmove the canoes and baggage, by the portage trail, to the foot of the\r\nfalls, and proceed on down the river. I shall take Drouillard and the\r\ntwo Fields, scout northward and strike the Maria\xe2\x80\x99s River, which I wish\r\nto follow down to the Missouri. I will meet you at the mouth of the\r\nMaria\xe2\x80\x99s River on the fifth day of August\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95if all goes well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, Cap\xe2\x80\x99n, do ye think three men\xe2\x80\x99ll be enough for ye?\xe2\x80\x9d blurted Pat.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe\xe2\x80\x99re goin\xe2\x80\x99 up where the bloody Big Bellies live. Give me Peter alone,\r\nan\xe2\x80\x99 take the rist. Peter an\xe2\x80\x99 I are plenty for this camp, till Ordway\r\ncomes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith Drouillard and the two Fields I\xe2\x80\x99ll stand off the Blackfeet,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nlaughed Captain Lewis. \xe2\x80\x9cEh, lads?\xe2\x80\x9d And he sobered. \xe2\x80\x9cIf my life is\r\nspared, Pat, I\xe2\x80\x99ll meet you on August 5. But if you don\xe2\x80\x99t hear from us,\r\nyou wait till the first day of September. Then if there\xe2\x80\x99s no word, you\r\nwill proceed on to Captain Clark at the mouth of the Yellowstone. Tell\r\nhim that my directions as commanding officer are for him to carry out\r\nour program and return to the United States, for I and my party have\r\nbeen destroyed. He already knows that I have planned this side trip to\r\nthe Maria\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPat saluted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYis, sorr. An\xe2\x80\x99, sorr (his voice was husky), I hope to meet ye safe an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsound at the mouth o\xe2\x80\x99 the Maria\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next morning, which was July 16, the captain took Drouillard, and\r\nthe two Fields, and six horses, and rode away, for the upper Maria\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nRiver in the country of the Gros-ventres of the Prairie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, boys,\xe2\x80\x9d spoke Pat; \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99re now siven men an\xe2\x80\x99 four hosses, an\xe2\x80\x99 we\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbetter be busy fixin\xe2\x80\x99 the carts an\xe2\x80\x99 trainin\xe2\x80\x99 the hosses to drag \xe2\x80\x99em,\r\nferninst the day when Ordway arrives with the canoes. I\xe2\x80\x99ve no fancy for\r\nplayin\xe2\x80\x99 hoss myself, when we\xe2\x80\x99ve got the rale animals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing especial happened, except the mosquitoes, until the arrival of\r\nSergeant Ordway and party. One trip was made to the lower end of the\r\nportage, to examine the white pirogue, and the caches; they all were\r\nsafe. Harness was manufactured, out of elk hide, for attaching the\r\nhorses to the wagons.\r\n\r\nSergeant Ordway appeared at three o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the afternoon of July\r\n19. He had with him Colter, Cruzatte, Collins, Potts, Lepage, Howard,\r\nWillard, Whitehouse, and Peter Wiser; the six canoes that had been sunk\r\nin the Jefferson River, and most of the goods that had been buried in\r\nthe cache, when last August the company under Captain Lewis had set out\r\nto follow Chief Ca-me-ah-wait to the Sho-sho-ne camp on the other side\r\nof the pass. Nothing had been stolen or injured.\r\n\r\nThe Sergeant Ordway party had separated from Captain Clark and party at\r\nthe Three Forks, and had come on down without adventure. The captain\r\nprobably was now on his way down the Yellowstone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 how were Sa-ca-ja-we-a an\xe2\x80\x99 the little spalpeen?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Pat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine and hearty. The Bird-woman said she knew the way to the\r\nYellowstone. She\xe2\x80\x99d been all through that country, when the Sho-sho-nes\r\nhunted the buffalo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the canoes were loaded upon the carts, the horses pulled very\r\nwell, for buffalo-horses; but, just as a year ago, the rain and the mud\r\ninterfered, the carts broke; besides, Pat was taken ill; so that five\r\ndays were required for carrying canoes and baggage around the series of\r\nfalls, to the old Portage Creek camp at the lower end.\r\n\r\nOne canoe was worthless, but the others were placed in the water; so\r\nwas the white pirogue; the blunderbuss or swivel cannon was unearthed\r\nand mounted in its bows, as before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, we\xe2\x80\x99re gettin\xe2\x80\x99 all our plunder together, wance ag\xe2\x80\x99in,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncongratulated Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 there\xe2\x80\x99s more of it, an\xe2\x80\x99 the red pirogue,\r\nremember, at the mouth o\xe2\x80\x99 the Maria\xe2\x80\x99s, where we\xe2\x80\x99re to meet Cap\xe2\x80\x99n Lewis.\r\nDo you be takin\xe2\x80\x99 the canoes down, Ordway, an\xe2\x80\x99 Peter an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99ll ride by\r\nland with the hosses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe mouth of the Maria\xe2\x80\x99s was not far\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95fifty miles by river, according\r\nto Pat\xe2\x80\x99s journal, written on the way up, but less by land. The Maria\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nas Peter recalled, was the fork of the Missouri where camp had been\r\nmade while the captains debated which route led to the Columbia.\r\nCaptain Lewis had explored up the Maria\xe2\x80\x99s and he and Captain Clark had\r\ndecided that the other fork was the right channel\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95the \xe2\x80\x9ctrue\xe2\x80\x9d Missouri.\r\n\r\nPeter and Pat covered thirty miles this first day. They saw thousands\r\nof buffalo, and a pack of wolves chasing an antelope. Pat shot an\r\nantelope, with his rifle, and Peter killed a buffalo with his arrows;\r\nthe next morning they killed, together, six antelope and seven\r\nbuffalo\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95which was all the meat that they could pack, although, as\r\ndeclared Pat, they might have killed a hundred.\r\n\r\nShortly after noon they came in sight of the mouth of the Maria\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nSergeant Ordway\xe2\x80\x99s party with the canoes already were there, and ashore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 ain\xe2\x80\x99t that Drouillard, too?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cYis! An\xe2\x80\x99 the cap\xe2\x80\x99n,\r\nb\xe2\x80\x99gorry! An\xe2\x80\x99 the two Fieldses! Somethin\xe2\x80\x99 must have fetched \xe2\x80\x99em back in\r\na hurry. \xe2\x80\x99Tis only July 28; they\xe2\x80\x99re a week ahead o\xe2\x80\x99 time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe quickened his horse into a trot, and leading each a horse packed\r\nhigh with meat and hides, he and Peter hastened forward to learn the\r\nnews.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXX\r\n\r\nCAPTAIN LEWIS MEETS THE ENEMY\r\n\r\n\r\nThe party seemed to be overhauling the cache here as if in a great\r\nhurry to go on; but the captain waved greeting, and Joe Fields\r\nstraightened up, to grin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYez got back mighty quick,\xe2\x80\x9d accused Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t yez go? An\xe2\x80\x99 where are\r\nthe hosses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure we went,\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Joe. \xe2\x80\x9cHosses? We\xe2\x80\x99ve turned \xe2\x80\x99em loose, of\r\ncourse; and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be turnin\xe2\x80\x99 yours loose, too, in a minute. So tumble\r\noff and I\xe2\x80\x99ll help you unpack. There\xe2\x80\x99s no time to waste. You ought to\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen along, Pat. We had a beautiful brush with the Injuns.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I tell yez?\xe2\x80\x9d reminded Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cAnnywan hurt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone of us. We wiped two of them out, though\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and a ball cut the\r\ncaptain\xe2\x80\x99s ha\xe2\x80\x99r. \xe2\x80\x99Twas this way,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Joe, as he tugged at a rope\r\nend, to release the pack of meat: \xe2\x80\x9cOn the fust day, \xe2\x80\x99fore we\xe2\x80\x99d gone\r\nmore\xe2\x80\x99n twenty mile from the falls, we struck Injun sign in shape of\r\na wounded-buffler trail; and after that we kept guard all night, for\r\nfear of our hosses. When we got to the Maria\xe2\x80\x99s we turned down, after\r\nscoutin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99round a bit. Found a lot of old Injun lodges, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nany Injuns till the 26th. Then the cap\xe2\x80\x99n sighted a bunch o\xe2\x80\x99 hosses,\r\nthirty of \xe2\x80\x99em, through his spy-glass\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and next several Injuns, on a\r\nhill, lookin\xe2\x80\x99 at Drouillard, who was across the river.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Bout half the hosses were saddled, which meant more Injuns somewhere\r\nnear. Our hosses were too tuckered to run far, and of course we\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t leave Drouillard; so the cap\xe2\x80\x99n said: \xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99ll go right on\r\nto those Injuns, boys; put on a bold front, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll have it out\r\nwith \xe2\x80\x99em. Don\xe2\x80\x99t let \xe2\x80\x99em think we\xe2\x80\x99re afraid. They may not be the\r\nGros-vent\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x99 When the Injuns fust saw us comin\xe2\x80\x99, they acted like\r\nthey were more afraid of us than we were of them. But we finally got\r\ntogether, the cap\xe2\x80\x99n made the peace sign, and told \xe2\x80\x99em our other man\r\nhad the pipe and after he\xe2\x80\x99d come in we\xe2\x80\x99d smoke. So Reub and one of the\r\nInjuns went after Drouillard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were only eight of \xe2\x80\x99em. They were the Big-bellies, all right,\r\nbut they had nothin\xe2\x80\x99 except two guns, and clubs and bows and arrers. We\r\nthought we could take care of ourselves; and that night we all camped\r\ntogether. The cap\xe2\x80\x99n told us in case of trouble to stick up and keep\r\ntogether and save the baggage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe slept in the same lodge with \xe2\x80\x99em. The cap\xe2\x80\x99n had given three of \xe2\x80\x99em\r\na flag and a medal and a handkerchief; but he put Reub on guard for\r\nthe night, and told him to watch sharp and wake us quick, so\xe2\x80\x99s to look\r\nafter the hosses, if the Injuns tried to sneak out. He and Drouillard\r\nlay down with the Injuns, and Reub and I stayed at the fire in the\r\nlodge entrance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went to sleep. Just at sunrise I woke up with a jump. Reub had\r\nyelled\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and there was an Injun runnin\xe2\x80\x99 off with my gun and his, and\r\nReub in chase. Drouillard was up and yellin\xe2\x80\x99, too\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x98Let go my gun! Let\r\ngo my gun!\xe2\x80\x99 he bawled, and I see him wrestlin\xe2\x80\x99 with another Injun, and\r\nthe cap\xe2\x80\x99n aimin\xe2\x80\x99 at another with his pistol. But I had to have my gun,\r\nso I ran after Reub and the fust Injun. Before I got there, Reub had\r\ncaught him and knifed him, and had both guns. Drouillard had his gun by\r\nthis time, and all the Injuns came pourin\xe2\x80\x99 out of the lodge, makin\xe2\x80\x99 for\r\nthe hosses, with the cap\xe2\x80\x99n and his pistol followin\xe2\x80\x99 the third Injun.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe drew a bead on the fellow, but he dropped the cap\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99s gun, and\r\nthe cap\xe2\x80\x99n wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let us shoot. \xe2\x80\x98Look out for those other rascals!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhe ordered. \xe2\x80\x98They\xe2\x80\x99re trying to drive off the hosses!\xe2\x80\x99 So Reub and\r\nDrouillard and I ran after six who were roundin\xe2\x80\x99 up the most of the\r\nhosses; and the cap\xe2\x80\x99n set out after his Injun and another who were\r\ndrivin\xe2\x80\x99 away a bunch. He made \xe2\x80\x99em leave twelve, but they kept on, with\r\nhis hoss, and that he was bound to get. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t have his bullet pouch\r\nor his hat; and when they were just \xe2\x80\x99bout to disappear in a little\r\ngully he told \xe2\x80\x99em to surrender the hoss or he\xe2\x80\x99d fire. With that they\r\nturned on him, and fire he did, downin\xe2\x80\x99 one of \xe2\x80\x99em slick as a whistle,\r\nbut the fellow had life enough to fire back an\xe2\x80\x99 sent a ball through the\r\ncap\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99s ha\xe2\x80\x99r.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe cap\xe2\x80\x99n had only his pistol, now, so he quit, and the other Injun\r\nmade off with the hoss. Drouillard had turned back to help the\r\ncap\xe2\x80\x99n, but Reub and I follered our Injuns till we got four of our own\r\ncritters, and then we let the rest go. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t matter, \xe2\x80\x99cause there were\r\nthe twelve left by the Injuns, so we\xe2\x80\x99d come out ahead in the little\r\ngame. Besides, we had the lodge, four shields, two bows and quivers,\r\nand a gun. Likewise the flag we\xe2\x80\x99d given, and the medal\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95but we left the\r\nmedal on the neck of the Injun Reub had killed, so as to show what kind\r\nof people we were.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we didn\xe2\x80\x99t hang \xe2\x80\x99round there long, you bet. The Injuns had said\r\nthe main band was only a day and a half away, and when the cap\xe2\x80\x99n had\r\ninvited \xe2\x80\x99em to bring their chiefs to council he of course told \xe2\x80\x99em\r\nwhere our camp was\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95at the mouth of the Maria\xe2\x80\x99s. Now we were desperate\r\nafraid the Injuns\xe2\x80\x99d out-foot us and attack you-all at the river. We\r\ntook four best horses, and only what meat we could carry, rode a\r\nhundred miles, with an hour and a half of rest, camped at two in the\r\nmornin\xe2\x80\x99, then rode another twenty miles and struck Ordway comin\xe2\x80\x99 down\r\nwith the canoes. We got aboard and here we are\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95and the cap\xe2\x80\x99n is in a\r\npowerful hurry to join Cap\xe2\x80\x99n Clark below.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was true; for, as said Drouillard: \xe2\x80\x9cDose Blackfeet now will hold\r\nall white men as enemies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis cache had caved in, and much of the supplies had spoiled. The red\r\npirogue also was found to be worthless, except for its spikes. Captain\r\nLewis hustled the work of loading, the rest of the horses were turned\r\nloose, and down the river again voyaged all. Sergeant Ordway was in\r\ncharge of the five canoes, Sergeant Pat and squad had charge of the\r\nwhite pirogue, which was the flagship.\r\n\r\nA sharp lookout was kept for the Big Bellies on the banks. However,\r\nnothing happened. The mouth of the Yellowstone was several days ahead;\r\nand when it was reached, no Captain Clark or others of that party\r\nappeared in sight. When halt was made, to look for sign, traces of the\r\ncaptain\xe2\x80\x99s camp were found, and in the sand Lepage discovered the scrawl:\r\n\r\n    W. C. a few miles further down on right hand side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen was that written, Lepage, do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Captain Lewis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMebbe two, mebbe t\xe2\x80\x99ree day ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said Baptiste. \xe2\x80\x9cDe rain haf washed\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate, he\xe2\x80\x99s safe,\xe2\x80\x9d uttered the captain, with much satisfaction.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect the mosquitoes drove him out of here. Whew!\xe2\x80\x9d For the\r\nmosquitoes were worse than ever. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll overtake him to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut they did not overtake the captain\xe2\x80\x99s party on the morrow, nor on the\r\nnext day. On the third day, which was August 11, the canoes stopped to\r\ntake aboard some meat; the white pirogue continued on, until Captain\r\nLewis espied a herd of elk in some willow brush, near the shore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurn in, boys,\xe2\x80\x9d he bade. \xe2\x80\x9cWait here. Come on, Cruzatte. We\xe2\x80\x99ll get a\r\nfew of those fellows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOut he leaped, gun in hand; and he and One-eyed Cruzatte disappeared in\r\nthe brush.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFaith, let\xe2\x80\x99s hope there aren\xe2\x80\x99t Injuns there, too,\xe2\x80\x9d quoth Sergeant Pat.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a likely place for an ambush.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHardly stands to reason there\xe2\x80\x99d be elk whar there are Injuns,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nremarked Alec Willard.\r\n\r\nEverybody waited anxiously; gazed and listened. Two rifle-shots were\r\nheard, distant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s meat, I reckon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alec.\r\n\r\nPresently another shot; and in about ten minutes out from the willow\r\nbrush and to the sandy shore burst Captain Lewis. He was running,\r\nlimping, staggering\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95he\xe2\x80\x99d been wounded\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95the left thigh of his leather\r\nbreeches was stained red!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo your arms, boys!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sergeant Pat.\r\n\r\nCaptain Lewis staggered on, to the white pirogue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been shot, men,\xe2\x80\x9d he panted. \xe2\x80\x9cNot mortally, I think. Indians are\r\nin that thicket. Cruzatte is somewhere there, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see any Injuns, cap\xe2\x80\x99n?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; the ball came from ambush, just as I was aiming at an elk. Gass,\r\ntake the men and follow me. We must rescue Cruzatte. I\xe2\x80\x99d lost sight of\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWillard, you and the two Fields,\xe2\x80\x9d roared Pat, springing into the\r\nshallows. \xe2\x80\x9cThe bloody Big-bellies ag\xe2\x80\x99in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Peter went also, with his bow and arrows. Nobody objected. The\r\ncaptain led on for about one hundred steps, when his leg gave out and\r\nhe almost fell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t travel,\xe2\x80\x9d he gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll return to the boat. If you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\noverpowered, Sergeant, keep your men together and retreat in good\r\norder, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll fight from the river.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYis, sorr.\xe2\x80\x9d And Pat gallantly plunged ahead, into the brush. \xe2\x80\x9cKentucky\r\nan the Irish ag\xe2\x80\x99in the redskins, lads,\xe2\x80\x9d he cheered. \xe2\x80\x9cBut mind your\r\neyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was exciting. The willows were thick\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95good hiding-place. Where was\r\nCruzatte\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95poor old Cruzatte with the one eye? Peter stuck close behind\r\nPat. His nostrils were wide, his eyes roved, his every sense was on the\r\nalert. He was Oto once more. Now was heard a crashing, before. Elk?\r\nIndian? Hah!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a mighty quare sort o\xe2\x80\x99 Injun, to be makin\xe2\x80\x99 all that noise,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nmuttered Pat, peering, his rifle advanced at a ready.\r\n\r\nAnd through a little open space here came Cruzatte! He was striding\r\nalong, with stained hands, his rifle on his shoulder, making for the\r\nboats and plainly much satisfied with himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHist!\xe2\x80\x9d said Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cCruzatte! \xe2\x80\x99Asy now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCruzatte started, and crouched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave ye seen Injuns?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNon,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Cruzatte. \xe2\x80\x9cI shoot one elk, follow \xe2\x80\x99nodder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome back to the boats with us, an\xe2\x80\x99 step lively,\xe2\x80\x9d ordered Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\r\nbe Injuns \xe2\x80\x99round. They shot the cap\xe2\x80\x99n in the leg.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy gracious!\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Cruzatte. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I see no sign.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNayther do we. Sure, it\xe2\x80\x99s powerful suspicious,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Pat.\r\n\r\nThey found the captain all prepared to defend himself in the pirogue.\r\nHe had laid out his rifle, pistol and pike, and was propped behind the\r\nair-gun that could shoot forty times.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you discover?\xe2\x80\x9d he challenged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a thing, sorr,\xe2\x80\x9d reported Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 Cruzatte, here, knows no more\r\nabout the Injuns than the rist of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere have you been, Cruzatte?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shoot wan elk, same time you shoot. Den I see nodder in brush, I\r\nshoot at heem, he vaneesh an\xe2\x80\x99 I try to find heem, but he get away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you did! How much of him did you see when you shot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cB\xe2\x80\x99gorry, you shot the cap\xe2\x80\x99n!\xe2\x80\x9d bellowed Sergeant Pat. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what you\r\ndid. Ye\xe2\x80\x99re blind as a mole! B\xe2\x80\x99gorry, you shot the cap\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95ye shot your\r\ncommandin\xe2\x80\x99 officer, an\xe2\x80\x99 by that ye\xe2\x80\x99re to be coortmartialed an\xe2\x80\x99 shot\r\nyourself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNon, non!\xe2\x80\x9d wailed old Cruzatte, wringing his hands. \xe2\x80\x9cI no mean to\r\nshoot heem. I see wan leetle brown spot in brush\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95look jus\xe2\x80\x99 like wan\r\nelk-fur, long way off; I take aim, bang!\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95I t\xe2\x80\x99ink I see elk run, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nI run to ketch heem. I no mean to shoot my capitaine. It wan grand\r\nmistake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you hear me call?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded the captain. \xe2\x80\x9cI suspected maybe\r\nthat ball came from your rifle and I hallooed as loud as I could. Why,\r\nby the shock you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been more than forty paces!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hear notting. I hear not one word,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Cruzatte.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe ball coming from so close, and you not answering, I of course\r\nthought of Indians,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the captain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cB\xe2\x80\x99gorry, give me wan chance at him an\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99ll close his other eye,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nbesought Pat; and all the men murmured angrily, while poor Cruzatte\r\nshivered with fright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI no mean to shoot my capitaine,\xe2\x80\x9d he babbled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, men,\xe2\x80\x9d said the captain. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was an error. My leather\r\nbreeches are just the shade of an elk hide, remember. Let\xe2\x80\x99s dress the\r\nwound. I doubt if it\xe2\x80\x99s serious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe ball had passed clear through his left thigh, and had furrowed the\r\nright; but it seemed not to have touched the bone or any artery. After\r\nthe wounds had been dressed and lint stuffed into the holes, the canoes\r\nwith the other elk hunters arrived; and not waiting to explain much the\r\ncaptain insisted upon them all pushing along, to catch up with Captain\r\nClark.\r\n\r\nNow that he himself was laid up, this was more necessary than before.\r\nAll he could do was to rest, half sitting, in the stern of the white\r\npirogue. His leg had so stiffened that he could scarcely move it.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXI\r\n\r\nTHE HOME STRETCH\r\n\r\n\r\nCaptain Clark was safe and well, with all his men, and only a short\r\ndistance down river! This was learned the next day from two white\r\ntrappers\xe2\x80\x95\xe2\x80\x95the first Americans met in over a year. Their names were\r\nHancock and Dickson. They had left Illinois, of the United States, in\r\nthe summer of 1804, and had been trapping in the upper Missouri country\r\never since.\r\n\r\nThey said that Captain Clark\xe2\x80\x99s party had passed them yesterday, but\r\nhad lost all the horses, by Indians, and were traveling in two wooden\r\ncanoes and two hide canoes. The captain had the idea that Captain Lewis\r\nand party were ahead of him.\r\n\r\nTrappers Hancock and Dickson had other news, also. They had seen the\r\nbarge, under Corporal Warfington, on its way from Fort Mandan, last\r\nsummer, to St. Louis. All aboard were well. Brave Raven, the Arikara\r\nchief, was there, bound for Washington; and so were several Yankton\r\nSioux chiefs, with old Pierre Dorion. But the Mandans and Minnetarees\r\nwere at war with the Arikaras; and the Mandans and the Assiniboines\r\nwere at war, too; and the Sioux were \xe2\x80\x9cbad.\xe2\x80\x9d So that the peace talks by\r\nthe captains had not buried the hatchet very deep.\r\n\r\nAnyway, soon after noon, this day, Captain Clark\xe2\x80\x99s camp was sighted,\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter here?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Captain Clark, the instant that the\r\npirogue grounded. He saw Captain Lewis lying in the stern.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing serious, Will. Merely a gun wound, in the thigh.
